Give me a translation and commentary. INCIPIT LIBER DE MYSTICA THEOLOGIA.
CAPITULUM I. De mystica theologia.
Trinitas superessentialis, et superdeus, et superoptime Christianorum inspector theosophiae, dirige nos in mysticorum eloquiorum superincognitum et superlucentem et sublimissimum verticem, ubi nova et absoluta et inconversibilia theologiae mysteria, secundum superlucentem absconduntur occulte docentis silentii caliginem, in obscurissimo, quod est supermanifestissimum, supersplendentem, et in qua omne relucet, et invisibilium superbonorum splendoribus superimplentem invisibiles intellectus. Mihi quidem haec opto. Tu autem, o amice Timothee, circa mysticas speculationes corroborato itinere et sensus desere, et intellectuales operationes, et sensibilia, et invisibilia, et omne non ens, et ens; et ad unitatem, ut possibile, inscius restituere ipsius, qui est super omnem essentiam et scientiam. Ea enim teipso et omnibus immensurabili et absoluto pure mentis excessu ad superessentialem divinarum tenebrarum radium, omnia deserens et ab omnibus absolutus ascendes. His autem, vide, quomodo nemo indoctorum auscultet. Indoctos autem dico, in his, quae sunt, conformatos, et nihil super existentia superessentialiter esse imaginantes. Sed his quidem hi, quos videre ea, quae secundum seipsos est, scientia oportet tenebras latibulum ejus. Si autem super hos sunt divinae in mysteria introductiones, quid quis dixerit de aliis ardentibus, quicunque omnibus superpositam causam ex ipsis in his, quae sunt, novissimis characterizant, et nihil eam superare aiunt ab ipsis fictarum impietatum et multiformium formationum? In ipsa etiam oportet omnes existentium ponere et affirmare positiones, veluti omnium causa, et omnes eas potentius negare, tanquam super omnia superexistente, et non aestimari depulsiones oppositas esse intentionibus, sed multo prius ipsam super privationes esse, quae est super omnem ablationem et positionem. Sic igitur divinus Bartholomaeus ait, et multam theologiam esse, et minimam, et evangelium latum, et magnum, et iterum correptum. Mihi videtur supernaturaliter intelligens, quia et multiloqua est optima omnium causa, et breviloqua simul, et sine verbo, quomodo neque verbum, neque intelligentiam habet, eo quod omnibus ipsa superessentialiter superposita est, et solis incircumvelate et vere manifesta, polluta omnia et immunda transgredientibus, et omnem omnium sanctarum summitatum ascensionem superascendentibus, et omnia divina lumina et sonos et verba caelestia superantibus, et in caliginem occidentibus, ubi vere est, ut eloquia aiunt, omnium summitas. Etenim non simpliciter divinus ipse Moyses primus mundari jubetur, et iterum ab his, qui tales non sunt, segregari, et post omnem purgationem audit multivocas tubas, et videt luminaria multa aperte fulgurantia, et multum fusos radios. Deinde multis segregatur, et cum electis sacerdotibus in summitatem divinarum ascensionum praecurrit: et si eis sic manentibus fit Deo, contemplatur vero non ipsum, invisibilis enim, sed locum ubi stetit. Hoc autem arbitror significare divinissima et sublimissima visibilium et intelligibilium, ypotheticos quosdam esse sermones, subjectorum omnia superanti, per quae super omnem intelligentiam ipsius praesentia ostenditur, intelligibilibus summitatibus sanctissimorum ejus locorum supergrediens: et quod ipsis absolvitur visibilibus et videntibus, et in caliginem ignorantiae occidit vere mysticam, per quam docet omnes gnosticas receptiones, in qua omne relucet, et invisibili innascitur omnis, qui est in omnium summitate, et a nullo, neque a seipso, neque altero, omnino autem ignoto omni scientia in otio per id quod melius est intellectus, et nihil cognoscendum super animum sic cognoscentium.

CAPITULUM II. Quomodo oportet et uniri et hymnos referre omnium causali et super omnia.
Juxta hanc nos fieri superlucentem oramus caliginem, et per invisibilitatem et ignorantiam videre et cognoscere ipsum super Deum et scientiam. Hoc non videre et scire, idipsum est vere videre et cognoscere, superessentialem superessentialiter laudare per omnium existentium ablationem, sicut per seipsum naturale agalma facientes, auferunt ea, quae superadjecta sunt, pura occulti visione vetantia, et ipsam in seipsa ablatione sola occultam manifestant formam. Oportet autem, ut arbitror, ablationes in contrarium positionibus laudare. Etenim illas quidem a praestantissimis inchoantes, et per media in novissima descendentes, apponimus. Hinc vero a novissimis ad principalissimas ascensiones facientes, omnia auferimus, ut incircumvelate cognoscamus illam ignorantiam, ab omnibus ignorantibus in omnibus existentibus circumvelatam, et superessentialem illam videamus caliginem, ab omni in existentibus luce occultatam.

CAPITULUM III. Quae sunt καταφατικαί theologiae, quae αποφατικαί.
In theologicis igitur characteribus potentissima affirmativae theologiae laudavimus, quomodo divina et optima natura unica dicitur, quomodo triadica, quae secundum ipsam dicta et paternitas, et filiolitas, quid vult declarare in spiritu theologia, quomodo ex immateriali et impartibili optimo in corde bonitatis germinata sunt lumina, et quomodo ipsius in ipso et in seipsis et inter se invicem coaeternae in germinatione mansionis servaverunt reditum, quomodo superessentialis Jesus humanis naturalibus veritatibus essentia factus est, et quaecunque alia ab eloquiis expressa sunt secundum theologicos characteres. In eo autem qui est de divinis nominibus, quomodo optimus nominatur, quomodo ων, quomodo vita, et sapientia, et virtus, et quaecunque alia intelligibilis sunt divinae nominationis. In symbolica vero theologia, quae sint a sensibilibus in divina transnominationes, quae divinae formae, quae divinae figurae, et partes, et organa, qui divini loci et mundi, qui furores, quae tristitiae et maniae, quae ebrietates et crapulae, quae juramenta, quae execrationes, qui somni, quae vigiliae, et quaecunque aliae symbolicae sunt divinae similitudinis sacre figuratae formationes. Et te arbitror considerasse, quomodo verbis copiosiora magis sunt novissima primis. Etenim habere theologicos characteres, et divinorum nominum reserationem breviorem verbis esse symbolica theologia. Quoniam quidem quantum ad superiora respicimus, tantum verba contemplationibus invisibilium coartantur: sicut et nunc in ipsam super intellectum occidentes caliginem, non brevem sermonem, sed sermonis defectum et nominationis inveniemus. Et ibi quidem desursum ad novissima descendens sermo, juxta quantitatem ejus, quae est universaliter ad proportionalem multitudinem, inventus est. Nunc autem ab his, quae deorsum sunt, ad superpositum ascendens, secundum mensuram invii corripitur, et post omne invium totus sine voce erit, et totus adunabitur sono carenti. Quare autem omnino, inquis, ex praestantissimo ponentes divinas positiones, a novissimis inchoamus divinam ablationem? Quia quid super omnem ponentes positionem, ex magis ipsi cognatiori conditionalem affirmationem oportuit ponere: quod autem super omnem ablationem auferentes, ex magis ipso distantibus auferre. An non magis est vita et bonitas, quam aer et lapis? Et magis non crapulae, et non maniae, quam non dicitur neque intelligitur?

CAPITULUM IV. Quia nihil sensibilium omnis sensibilis per excellentiam causalis.
Dicamus igitur sic: Omnium causa, et super omnia ens, neque carens essentia est, neque carens vita, neque irrationabilis est, neque insensualis, neque corpus est, neque figura, neque species, neque qualitatem, aut quantitatem, aut tumorem habet, neque in loco est, neque videtur, neque tactum sensibilem habet, neque sentitur, neque sensibilis est, neque inordinationem habet, neque perturbationem a passionibus materialibus commota, neque impotens est sensibilibus succumbens casibus, neque indigens est lucis, neque mutationem, aut corruptionem, aut partitionem, aut privationem, aut fluxum, neque aliud quid sensibilium est, neque habet

CAPITULUM V. Quia nihil intelligibilium omnis intelligibilis per excellentiam causalis.
Iterum autem ascendentes dicamus, ων neque anima est, neque intellectus, neque phantasiam, aut opinionem, aut verbum, aut intelligentiam habet, neque ratio est, neque intelligentia, neque dicitur, neque intelligitur, neque numerus est, neque ordo, neque magnitudo, neque parvitas, neque aequalitas, neque similitudo aut dissimilitudo, neque stat, neque movetur, neque silentium ducit, neque habet virtutem, neque virtus est, neque lux, neque vita est, neque hostia est, neque seculum, neque tempus, neque tactus est ejus intelligibilis, neque scientia, neque veritas est, neque regnum, neque sapientia, neque unum, neque unitas, neque deitas, aut bonitas, neque spiritus est, sicut nos scimus, neque filiolitas, neque paternitas, neque aliud quid nobis aut alicui existentium cognitum, neque quid non existentium, neque quid existentium est, neque existentia eam cognoscunt, an ipsa sit, neque ipsa cognoscit existentia, an existentia sunt, neque verbum ejus est, neque nomen, neque scientia, neque tenebrae est, neque lumen, neque error, neque veritas, neque est ejus universaliter positio, neque ablatio, sed eorum, quae post eam sunt, positiones et ablationes facientes, ipsam neque auferimus, neque ponimus, quoniam et super omnem positionem est perfecta et singularis omnium causa, super omnem ablationem excellentia omnium simpliciter perfectione, et summitas omnium.

Assistant

Below is an English‐translation of the passage you provided—with one possible rendering of its notoriously dense and “baroque” mystical Latin—and a commentary explaining some of its themes and stylistic features. Note that the original text is highly complex, full of layered adjectives (especially the repeated “super‑” terms), and its syntax is intentionally obscure in order to evoke a transcendent mystery. There is no single “correct” translation, but the version below aims to convey both the literal sense and the flavor of its mystical language.

Translation

Incipit Liber de Mystica Theologia


(Here begins the Book of Mystical Theology.)

Chapter I. On Mystical Theology

Translation:


“O Trinity of the super‐essence, and super‑God, and the most excellent inspector of Christian theosophy, direct us toward that mystical peak of discourse which is beyond common knowledge—shining and most sublime—where new, absolute, and incommunicable mysteries of theology, in accordance with the radiant hidden (or ‘super‐radiant’) secret of the teaching silence’s darkness, are concealed. This darkness is the most obscure yet is also the most manifest and most resplendent, wherein all things shine, and wherein the invisible intellects are filled with the super‑splendors of invisible goodness. Such things I indeed desire.


But you, O friend Timothee, having been strengthened by your journey and senses in mystical speculations, must abandon both intellectual operations and those things sensible and insensible, as well as both non‑being and being; and you must, as far as possible, restore that unity of which he is the source—the one who is above every essence and all knowledge.


For by this very self and by all that is immeasurable and the absolutely pure excess of the mind, you abandon all things and ascend absolutely, separate from everything, to the super‑essential ray of divine darkness.


And behold how none of the unlearned listens to these matters. To the unlearned I say that these things are conformed to that which exists, and that they imagine nothing beyond existence in a super‑essential manner.


Yet regarding those who see that which is, their knowledge must contain the hiding-place of that darkness. But if there are divine introductions into the mysteries for these, then what shall one say of the others who burn with zeal—those who characterize everything with a super‑imposed cause, claiming that nothing can surpass that cause except the imputed myriad of false pieties and various forms?


In that very thing it is necessary to establish and affirm the positions of all existing things—as if they were the cause of everything—and then to deny them even more powerfully, as though the super‑existing were above all, and not to regard the opposing pushes of removal as intentions but rather as pre‑existing privations which are above every removal and positioning.


Thus, the divine Bartholomew says, there is much theology, and little theology, and the Gospel is broad, and great, and again corrected.


It seems to me that he who understands supernaturally, for he is the best of all causes in many words yet brief at the same time and without utterance—just as he has neither word nor understanding—because all things are super‑imposed in a super‑essential way, and with only the circumambient and truly manifest, he overwhelms and defiles all that transgresses, and causes all the highest ascensions of the sanctified to surpass, and all the divine lights, sounds, and heavenly words to overcome, and in the setting darkness—where, truly, as they say, the highest of all is manifest.


For it is not simply that the divine himself, as Moses, is commanded to be purified first, and again separated from those who are not such, and after all purification he hears many-voiced trumpets, and sees many lights shining forth openly, and many rays dispersed.


Then he is separated into many parts, and he precedes with the elect priests into the summit of divine ascensions; and if in such a state they remain with God, then he is contemplated not as the invisible itself but as the place where he once stood.


This, I judge, signifies that the most divine and highest of the visible and intelligible—the hypothetical words of certain subjects that overcome everything—through which the very presence of him is revealed beyond all understanding, surpassing even the intelligible summits of his most sanctified places—is what is expiated by the visible and the seers, and in the darkness of ignorance a true mysticism dies; by this mysticism all Gnostic receptions are taught, in which everything shines, and the invisible is born in everyone who is in the highest of all, and by none—not from itself, nor from another, but entirely unknown to all knowledge in repose by that which is the better part of the understanding—and nothing is to be known of the spirit by such knowing.”


— End of Chapter I

Chapter II. How All Things and Hymns Must Be United and Recounted as Causal and Over All

Translation:


“In accordance with this we pray that from the shining we be made, and through invisibility and ignorance we may see and know that which is above God and knowledge. Not to see and know this is to see and know truly—that is, to super‑essentially praise by the removal of all existing things—just as making for itself a natural image; it removes that which is super‑added by the pure, hidden vision that forbids, and in that very removal it alone reveals the hidden form.


Yet it is necessary, I believe, to praise removals in contrary positions. For indeed we set forth those which begin with the most excellent, and descending from the middle into the very last; from these, making ascensions from the last to the principal, we remove everything, so that by the circumambient ignorance we may know that ignorance, hidden by all ignorant things in all that exists, and see that super‑essential darkness, hidden by every light in existence.”


— End of Chapter II

Chapter III. Concerning the “Καταφατικαί” (Affirmative) and “Αποφατικαί” (Negative) Theology

Translation:


“In our discussion of the theological characters of the most powerful affirmative theology, we have extolled how the divine and best nature is said to be unique, how it is triadic according to what is called Fatherhood and Filiality, and what the Spirit of theology intends to declare; how, from that which is immaterial and indivisible, in the best heart of goodness, lights are generated; and how in him, within himself and in himself and among one another, they have preserved the perpetual return in the dwelling of germination. We have also explained how the super‑essential Jesus became essence to human natural truths, and whatever else has been expressed by eloquence according to the theological characters.


And in that which is concerning the divine names, we have shown how the best is named; how it is ὤν (being), how it is life, and wisdom, and virtue, and whatever else is intelligible in the divine designation.


In what may be called symbolic theology—that is, that which comes from the sensible into divine trans‑nominations—we have treated those divine forms, those divine figures, and the parts and organs of the divine place and world; which passions, which sorrows and manias, which inebriations and debaucheries, which oaths, which execrations, which dreams, which vigils, and whatever else are the sacredly figurated formations of divine likeness.


And I suppose you have considered how, with words, the more copious are newer than the earlier. For to have theological characters and the reservation of divine names in briefer words is what symbolic theology is.


For as far as we look toward that which is higher, the words are constrained by the contemplations of the invisible: just as now, when falling into the super‑intelligible darkness, we find not a brief discourse but rather a defect of discourse and of designation.


And indeed there, descending toward the very last, a speech is discovered according to its quantity, which is universal in proportion to the multitude; and now, ascending from that which is below to that which is super‑imposed, according to the measure of the invisible, it is corrected, and after all the invisible, all is without voice, and entirely united in a lack of sound.


Therefore, I ask you, why should we not, having established the divine positions in the most excellent manner, begin with the divine removal from the last? For what is there but that one should establish a conditional affirmation known to itself as super‑imposed on every position—and then, removing everything above all removals, one removes from that which is farthest away.


Is not life and goodness greater than air and stone? And are not inebriation and mania likewise not said nor understood?”


— End of Chapter III

Chapter IV. That Nothing of the Sensible Can Possess the Excellence of the Causal

Translation:


“Let us then say thus: The cause of all things, and above all beings, neither lacks essence nor life, is not irrational, nor insensible, nor is it a body, nor a figure, nor a species, nor does it have quality or quantity, nor any mass; it is neither in place nor appears, nor does it have any sensible touch, nor is it sensed, nor is it sensible; it has no ordering, nor is it disturbed by material passions, nor is it impotent before sensible events, nor does it lack light, nor undergo mutation, corruption, partition, privation, or flux, nor is there anything else of the sensible, nor does it have any.”


— End of Chapter IV

Chapter V. That Nothing of the Intelligible Can Possess the Excellence of the Causal

Translation:


“Again, ascending we say: That which is ὤν (being) has neither soul, nor intellect, nor imagination, nor opinion, nor word, nor understanding, nor reason; it has neither intelligence nor is it spoken of, nor is it understood, nor is it a number, nor an order, nor a magnitude, nor smallness, nor equality, nor likeness or unlikeness, nor is it static, nor does it move, nor does silence lead, nor does it have any power, nor is power itself; it is neither light nor life, nor a victim, nor an age, nor time, nor any sensible touch does its intelligible have, nor is it knowledge, nor truth, nor a kingdom, nor wisdom, nor one, nor unity, nor deity or goodness, nor is it spirit—as we know—nor filiality, nor paternity, nor anything else known to us or to any existing thing, nor of that which does not exist; nor is it that which exists, nor do existing things know it, or does it itself know existence, or are there things that exist in it; neither is its word nor name, nor is it knowledge, nor darkness, nor light, nor error, nor truth, nor is its universal position determined, nor is there removal—but rather those things that come after it, making positions and removals; for we neither remove nor establish it, since over every position it is perfect and singular as the cause of all things, over every removal it is the excellence of all in pure perfection, and the summit of all.”


— End of Chapter V

>
Explicit: The Book of Mystical Theology is ended.

Commentary

1. Language and Style

The text is written in a deliberately dense and “overloaded” style. The repeated use of intensifiers such as “super‑essential,” “super‑God,” “super‑excellent,” and “super‑imposed” serves both a poetic and a mystical function. In medieval mystical writing, such language was not merely ornamentation—it was meant to point beyond ordinary conceptual limits toward a transcendent mystery that can only be intuited or experienced, not fully expressed in ordinary language.

2. Mystical Themes

  • Transcendence and Ineffability:

The text repeatedly directs the reader to “ascend” from the realm of sensible and intellectual operations into a mode of direct, intuitive, and mystical knowing. It emphasizes that the ultimate divine reality is “beyond” both the categories of being and non‑being and beyond all forms of rational discourse. This reflects a typical mystical insistence that the divine is both immanent and utterly transcendent.

  • The Role of “Darkness” and “Light”:

Ironically, the text describes a “divine darkness” that conceals the incommunicable mysteries, even as it is “the most manifest” and “resplendent” form. In mystical literature, darkness is often not a negation but a symbol of the unknowable, hidden nature of God—a darkness that is itself luminous when approached from a higher, inner vision.

  • Affirmative vs. Negative Theology:

In Chapter III, the discussion of “καταφατικαί” (affirmative) and “αποφατικαί” (negative) theology outlines two approaches: one that speaks of God by asserting what He is (using positive, symbolic images such as life, wisdom, and virtue) and one that negates any limited, finite description of the divine. This dialectic is a hallmark of medieval mystical thought, notably found in the works of Pseudo‑Dionysius and later mystics.

3. The Process of Spiritual Ascent

Throughout the text, the language describes a process of “removal” (ablatio) and “positioning” whereby the mystic must shed all finite categories and all things super‑imposed upon the divine in order to approach the pure, unmediated divine essence. The imagery of ascending with the elect priests, hearing trumpets, and seeing dispersed rays recalls visionary experiences common in mystical literature and apophatic (negative) theology.

4. Theological and Philosophical Implications

  • Unity Beyond Dichotomies:

The text insists on a unity that transcends ordinary dichotomies such as being and non‑being, light and darkness, sensible and intelligible. In doing so, it points to a divine ground that is wholly “other” than anything we can categorize. This notion is central to mystical theology: God is the ultimate “One” who is both the cause of everything and beyond all contingent properties.

  • Critique of the Unlearned:

The writer warns that “none of the unlearned listens” to these mysteries, implying that such teachings are reserved for those who have undergone a certain spiritual or intellectual purification. This reflects a common medieval idea that true mystical knowledge is not accessible to everyone and that one must renounce ordinary conceptual frameworks to approach the divine.

5. Concluding Thoughts

The final chapters (IV and V) sharply differentiate between the sensible and the intelligible realms, arguing that neither realm, with all its properties and limitations, can contain the causal excellence of the divine. In other words, God is not to be found within any particular attribute (like life, light, or truth) as these are merely relative and finite; rather, God is the ground of all being and non‑being, a mystery that defies all ordinary description.

Summary

This text is a striking example of medieval mystical theology that seeks to describe the inexpressible nature of the divine. It employs an elaborate and highly technical vocabulary to instruct the reader in a method of negation—both of sensory and intellectual attributes—to ultimately arrive at a state of unity with the divine mystery. The commentary above aims to help modern readers appreciate both the poetic richness and the profound theological assertions of the original Latin.

Assistant

Below is a discussion that examines specific translation choices—word by word and phrase by phrase—and considers how they both echo and illuminate the metaphysical preoccupations found in John Scottus Eriugena’s work. In many ways, the translator’s decisions (whether consciously or by necessity) reveal an attitude toward reality that is at once rigorously systematic, paradoxical, and steeped in the language of negative theology.

1. The Proliferation of “Super‑” Adjectives

Translation Choices:

  • Phrases such as “super‑essence,” “super‑God,” “super‑excellent inspector,” and “super‑imposed” appear repeatedly in the text.

  • The translator renders Latin compounds like superessentialis, superdeus, and others by retaining the prefix “super‑,” rather than choosing more conventional English adjectives such as “transcendent” or “supreme.”


Metaphysical Implications:
  • Hyperbolic Precision and Overabundance: Eriugena (and similar medieval thinkers) used this elaborate series of adjectives to push language beyond its ordinary limits. In translating these compounds literally, the translator reveals an understanding that for Eriugena, the divine must be described in terms that exceed the capacities of everyday language.

  • Unity and Multiplicity: The repetition of “super‑” stresses a structure in which multiple levels or layers are superimposed upon a single absolute reality. This reflects Eriugena’s metaphysical interest in a God who is simultaneously immanent in, and transcendent to, all created forms. The translator’s insistence on preserving this adjective prefixing system underscores the tension between the finite and the infinite—a key theme in Eriugena’s work.


2. The Dual Imagery of Light and Darkness

Translation Choices:

  • Phrases like “mystical peak of discourse,” “radiant hidden,” “teaching silence’s darkness,” “most manifest and resplendent,” and “super‑essential darkness” are carefully chosen to convey paradoxical images.

  • The translator renders caliginem as “darkness,” but immediately pairs it with adjectives such as “resplendent” and “shining.”


Metaphysical Implications:
  • Paradox and Ineffability: Eriugena’s thought is famously paradoxical. The translation choices here reflect an underlying metaphysics in which darkness is not simply the absence of light but a dynamic, luminous mystery in its own right.

  • Apophatic Tendency: By maintaining the contradictory images (light/shining vs. darkness/hidden), the translator reveals a metaphysical orientation toward apophatic (negative) theology—the idea that God’s essence cannot be positively described but is encountered through what it is not. This careful balance between affirmation and negation is central to Eriugena’s thought.


3. Process and Movement: “Removals” and “Ascensions”

Translation Choices:

  • The translator frequently employs terms like “removals” (for Latin ablationes) and “ascensions” (for Latin words connoting upward movement) to describe the mystic’s journey.

  • Phrases such as “abandon all finite categories” and “ascend absolutely” are used to communicate a process of purification and detachment.


Metaphysical Implications:
  • Dynamic Process of Unknowing: Eriugena’s metaphysics involves a movement away from the created, toward the uncreated or absolute. The translator’s choices here reveal an understanding of this as a process—a journey in which one must systematically strip away every finite, conditioned attribute to approach the divine essence.

  • Interplay of Presence and Absence: By choosing words that emphasize both movement (ascension) and removal (ablation), the translator mirrors Eriugena’s view that divine reality is both immanent (present within all things) and transcendent (requiring one to remove or negate finite aspects to see it clearly).


4. The Distinction Between the Sensible and the Intelligible

Translation Choices:

  • In Chapters IV and V, the translator renders detailed lists of what the “sensible” and “intelligible” are not. For instance, in Chapter IV, phrases like “neither lacks essence nor life” and “is not irrational, nor insensible, nor is it a body” are rendered in a careful, almost litany-like enumeration.

  • Similarly, Chapter V’s long negative catalog—“neither soul, nor intellect, nor imagination, nor opinion…”—is kept as a methodical negation.


Metaphysical Implications:
  • Emphasis on Negative Theology: The translator’s commitment to these exhaustive negations reflects a metaphysical commitment to apophatic theology. Eriugena’s work often revolves around what God is not, as much as what God is; these passages underscore the point that the divine exceeds every category and attribute we can conceive.

  • Order Amid Complexity: The systematic negation also mirrors Eriugena’s own interest in the unity underlying the multiplicity of finite attributes. By listing and then negating these attributes, the translator helps the reader appreciate the rigor of Eriugena’s metaphysical system—a system that seeks to deconstruct every limited quality in order to reveal the singular, all-encompassing cause.


5. Word Choice Reflecting a Dialectical Attitude

Translation Choices:

  • The translator does not “smooth over” the tension between opposites; rather, he preserves the original’s dialectical quality by juxtaposing terms like “being and non‑being,” “sensible and insensible,” “affirmative and negative theology.”

  • Phrases such as “the super‑imposed cause” and “the excellence of all in pure perfection” are maintained to stress a metaphysical hierarchy that is both cumulative and non‑linear.


Metaphysical Implications:
  • Dialectical Synthesis: Eriugena’s metaphysics is dialectical—seeking synthesis in the interplay of opposites. The translator’s choices emphasize that one must grasp both sides of any dichotomy to approach the absolute. In doing so, the translation reveals an attitude that the divine is found in the tension and reconciliation of opposites, not in a simple, one-dimensional affirmation.

  • Uncompromising Complexity: By preserving the intricate language, the translator signals an admiration for, and commitment to, the intellectual complexity of Eriugena’s thought. The language is not “tamed” for clarity’s sake but left in its rigorous, almost challenging form, inviting the reader into a deeper metaphysical engagement.


Conclusion

The translator’s choices—whether in preserving the “super‑” adjectives, maintaining the paradoxical imagery of light and darkness, or methodically negating the attributes of both the sensible and the intelligible—are far from arbitrary. They serve to mirror Eriugena’s own metaphysical outlook: one that emphasizes a radical transcendency, the necessity of negation, and the dynamic process of ascension toward an ineffable divine unity. In this way, the translation not only communicates the text’s literal meaning but also reveals the translator’s own commitment to a metaphysical vision in which language is both a tool and a limit in the quest for the absolute.

Feel free to ask if you’d like to delve into specific phrases or further aspects of Eriugena’s metaphysics as reflected in the translation!

Tool

Tracing Eriugena's translation

I’m working through Eriugena’s Latin translation of Greek texts, noting his metaphysical outlook, neologisms, and syntactic innovations, especially in his "Liber de Mystica Theologia."

Examining Eriugena's Latin translation

Eriugena's Latin is a rich mixture of Greek intensifiers and Neoplatonic theology. Interestingly, he uses transliterated Greek terms for mystical concepts and compound adjectives like "superessentialis" to render the ineffable.

Assistant

Below is a discussion of how John Scottus Eriugena’s translation choices—from Greek into Latin—reflect not only his philological ingenuity but also his deeply held metaphysical convictions. Although English did not exist in his time, his rendering into Latin is itself a creative act that both preserves and transforms Greek theological and philosophical ideas. Here are some key points:

1. Innovative Compound Adjectives and Neologisms

Eriugena’s Latin Renderings:

  • Eriugena frequently employs compound adjectives—terms such as superessentialis, superdeus, and superoptime—that have no direct parallel in classical Latin.

  • Rather than opting for more conventional adjectives (for example, using supremus or excelsus in lieu of a “super‑” prefix), he forges new compounds that convey an intensified, layered quality.


Metaphysical and Philosophical Implications:
  • Capturing the Ineffable: In the Greek originals, many theological concepts are expressed with a nuance that resists reduction to simple adjectives. Eriugena’s inventive compounds aim to capture this complexity, suggesting that the divine, in its many aspects, exceeds ordinary categories of description.

  • Reflecting a Hierarchy of Being: The persistent use of the “super‑” prefix mirrors a metaphysical vision in which the divine is not merely above the finite but is structured in multiple, gradated layers of transcendence and immanence. His Latin compounds invite the reader to consider an order of reality where every attribute is “above” or “beyond” its usual limits—thus echoing the dialectical and apophatic tendencies of the original Greek thought.


2. Rendering Greek Dialectics into Latin Syntax

Eriugena’s Approach to Contrasts:

  • Greek texts, particularly those influenced by Neoplatonism and the later mystical tradition, often present reality in terms of paradox and contradiction—for instance, by juxtaposing light and darkness or affirmation and negation.

  • In his Latin translation, Eriugena preserves these dialectical tensions rather than “smoothing” them into a more homogenized style. For example, his careful rendering of both καταφατικαί (cataphatic) and αποφατικαί (apophatic) theology into Latin terms underscores the necessity of both affirming and negating divine attributes.


Metaphysical and Philosophical Implications:
  • Embracing Paradox: Eriugena’s Latin syntax, replete with balanced phrases and coordinated oppositions, is designed to reflect the Greek insistence that the divine transcends all dichotomy. His careful attention to the original’s structure reinforces his conviction that true knowledge of God requires both what can be said and what must be unsaid.

  • The Role of Negation: The methodical lists of what the sensible or the intelligible are not (seen in the exhaustive negatives of Chapters IV and V) are translated in a way that mirrors the Greek penchant for apophatic theology. This reinforces the idea that the ultimate reality is found beyond the confines of all positive descriptions.


3. Transliteration and the Preservation of Greek Terms

Eriugena’s Treatment of Greek Lexicon:

  • When encountering Greek terms with deep philosophical or theological significance—such as ὤν (being) or key technical terms—Eriugena often opts to transliterate rather than translate them into wholly Latin equivalents.

  • This approach acknowledges that certain concepts in Greek carry a philosophical heft that might be diminished if rendered too “domestically” in Latin vocabulary.


Metaphysical and Philosophical Implications:
  • Maintaining Philosophical Nuance: By preserving the sound and form of Greek words, Eriugena signals that these terms are not merely labels but encapsulations of a complex, inherited tradition. They invite the Latin reader into the original Greek intellectual world, where metaphysical categories are in constant interplay.

  • A Synthesis of Traditions: Eriugena’s translation is itself a synthesis—a melding of Greek philosophical rigor with the Latin theological tradition. His transliteration of key Greek words not only honors the source material but also creates a bridge that allows his Latin audience to access the subtleties of Greek metaphysics.


4. Language as a Reflective Tool for Mystical Ascent

Eriugena’s Use of Dynamic Language:

  • His rendering of movement, process, and transformation—using terms like ablationes (removals) and words denoting ascension—reflects the Greek vision of the soul’s dynamic journey toward union with the divine.

  • Eriugena’s language does not treat these processes as static or purely descriptive; rather, his syntactic choices themselves enact the process of “coming-to-be” and “passing-away,” mirroring the dialectical movement found in Greek mysticism.


Metaphysical and Philosophical Implications:
  • Process Over Fixed States: This approach emphasizes that for Eriugena, reality is not a series of fixed essences but a continuous process of transformation. The Latin language he chooses is fluid enough to convey this movement—suggesting that every term is provisional and every description is but a stepping stone toward the ineffable.

  • Embodied Language and the Divine: The very act of translation, in his hands, becomes an ascensional act—one that takes the reader from the tangible, sensible world toward a realm where the divine can be approached only through negation and metaphor.


Conclusion

Eriugena’s Latin translation of Greek mystical theology is much more than a linguistic exercise—it is a metaphysical statement. His choices in compound adjectives, his preservation of dialectical oppositions, his transliteration of crucial Greek terms, and his dynamic, process-oriented syntax all serve to reflect a deeply held conviction: that the divine transcends and permeates all finite categories. By rendering Greek thought in his own inventive Latin, Eriugena not only communicates the original ideas but also transforms them, creating a text that is as much a work of metaphysical art as it is of translation. This synthesis of language and philosophy reveals his commitment to a vision of reality that is at once systematic, paradoxical, and ultimately ineffable.

yanulada
This page intentionally left blank.

INTRODUCTION

This book is the fruit of more than twelve years’ study and teaching of the
thought of Dionysius the Areopagite,1 together with that of Plotinus and
Proclus, as philosophy: not, primarily, as a late antique cultural phenomenon;
nor as an influential episode in the history of Christian theology; nor as
“mysticism,” if that be taken to mean something other than philosophy; nor
as a series of texts with ascertainable relations of influence and citation; but
as philosophy, i.e. as a rationally justified, coherent account of the nature of
reality. Such a philosophical exposition of any body of thought demands
more than an explanation of what the philosopher says and of the sources
from which he derives his doctrines. It requires, above all, an account of the
argumentation, the sequence of reasoning that supports and leads to his
positions. Only by understanding this argumentation can we truly grasp the
meaning of the positions themselves.

In the case of Dionysius, such an understanding is particularly difficult
to achieve because he notoriously eschews argumentation in favor of
proclamatory exposition. (See Ep. VII.1, 1077B–1080A.) But that does not
mean that his thought is not open to philosophical interpretation and pre-
sentation. It simply means that we must look for its underlying argumenta-
tion elsewhere, in the philosophical tradition from which his thought derives.
To take a prime example, the central Dionysian doctrine that God is “be-
yond being” is not merely a phrase or a theme which has a discoverable
history in Plato and Neoplatonism, nor is it merely a vague assertion of
divine transcendence. Rather, within the Neoplatonic context, it is the
conclusion of a definite sequence of philosophical reasoning, and only in
terms of that argumentation can its precise meaning be correctly grasped.
The same is true of other characteristic Dionysian themes such as procession
and reversion, evil as privation, hierarchy, mystical union, and symbolism.
The textual “source” of a given idea in Dionysius may be Proclus, or Plotinus,
or some other writer, pagan or Christian; but its real philosophical origin is
a certain line of reasoning, and this is what I aim to bring to light.

The purpose of this study, therefore, is not to contribute to the extensive
Quellenforschung that has already been undertaken on Dionysius, but rather to
elucidate the meaning and grounds of his vision of reality by looking back
through the philosophical tradition to recover the structures and argumentation

Chapters 1 and 2 correspond in subject-matter to Divine Names I, dis-
cussing, in chapter 1, the radical transcendence and unknowability of God,
and, in chapter 2, the immanence and manifestation of God in all things.

Chapter 3 corresponds to Divine Names IV.1–17, presenting this doc-
trine in its dynamic aspect by discussing Dionysius’ account of God as
Goodness, Beauty, and Love in terms of the cycle of remaining, procession,
and reversion.

Chapter 4 corresponds to Divine Names IV.18–35, addressing the prob-
lem of evil as it arises from such an understanding of reality.

Chapter 5 corresponds to Divine Names V.1–3, discussing the hierar-
chical structuring of being in relation to the doctrine of being as theophany.

Chapter 6 corresponds to Divine Names VII, discussing the nature and
modes of cognition within such an understanding of being.

Chapter 7, finally, presents Dionysius’ philosophy of symbolism as it
emerges from this metaphysics and gnoseology, and is linked primarily with
Celestial Hierarchy I–II.

4 INTRODUCTION

In a manner that perhaps deliberately parallels his own doctrine of
divine names, the author of the Dionysian corpus remains invisible: he lies
hidden behind his works and can be known only as he is manifest in them,
so that the very name Dionysius inevitably refers to the content of the works
rather than to the author.12 The absence of biographical information about
the author encourages a reading of the works in purely philosophical terms,
simply as a body of thought. At the same time, perhaps in part because of his
pseudonymity, Dionysius has tended to be studied not sine but cum ira et studio,
and few expositions of his thought even make a pretense of neutrality.13 The
present study is no exception: my own love for Dionysius will be patent through-
out. But this love is accompanied by, or rather is one with, an equally great
love for Plato and Plotinus, and above all for divine philosophy itself.

CHAPTER ONE

BEYOND BEING AND INTELLIGIBILITY

In recent decades there has been a surge of interest in “negative theology,”1

of which Dionysius is a leading exponent, and hence many studies of this
feature of Dionysius’ thought.2 Rarely, however, do such studies attempt to
present the philosophical argumentation that underlies his teachings. The
doctrine that God or the One, the first principle of reality, lies beyond being
and beyond thought, for Dionysius and his Neoplatonic forebears, is not an
ungrounded starting point or an article of faith but rather the conclusion of
a rigorous sequence of philosophical reasoning, and only by following this
argumentation can we truly understand the doctrine’s meaning. Neoplatonic
and Dionysian “negative theology” and “mysticism” is an aspect of rational
metaphysics, and must be interpreted and evaluated as such. The aim of the
present chapter, therefore, is to expose the philosophical grounds and mean-
ing of Dionysius’ negative theology by showing how the argument behind it
is developed in the Greek philosophical tradition that Dionysius draws on
and continues.

【8†(S U N Y Series in Ancient Greek Philosophy) Eric David Perl - Theophany_ The Neoplatonic Philosophy of Dionysius the Areopagite (2007).pdf†file-Hkx1s3zBZGQpGFeaY8dqkn】


23. Dionysius’ view of evil as deficiency of being and hence as uncaused and
unintelligible is thus closely comparable to Augustine’s, although Augustine thinks
more of efficient causality and Dionysius more of final causality. Having traced the
origin of evil back as far as the will of a rational creature, Augustine then says, “Let
no one, then, seek an efficient cause of an evil will. For its cause is not efficient, but
deficient, because the evil will itself is not an effect of something, but a defect . . . Now
to seek the causes of these defections, which are . . . not efficient causes, but deficient,
is like wishing to see darkness or hear silence . . . Thus, too, our mind perceives
intelligible forms by understanding them; but when they are deficient, it knows them
by not knowing them; for ‘who can understand his failings?’ . . . [T]he will is made
evil by nothing else than the defection by which God is forsaken: a defection of
which the cause, too, is certainly deficient.” Augustine, The City of God against the
Pagans, ed. and tr. R. W. Dyson (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1998),
XII.7–9, 507–09.

V.3, 817AB.

NOTE ON TRANSLATIONS

There is still no adequate English translation of the Dionysian corpus. The
best, that of John Parker,1 is both highly imperfect and largely unavailable;
while the most recent and widely available, that of Colm Luibheid,2 is so far
from the Greek as to be almost a paraphrase rather than a translation, and
disregards Dionysius’ use of traditional philosophical terms. Hence I have
provided my own translations of passages cited from Dionysius, making them
as literal as possible without egregiously violating English usage.

16. Cf. A. H. Armstrong, “Negative Theology, Myth, and Incarnation,” in
Neoplatonism and Christian Thought, ed. Dominic O’Meara (Albany: State University
of New York Press, 1982), 217: “It seems that the traditional terms ‘beyond being,’
‘nonbeing,’ or ‘nothing’ applied to God are most significant when used in their proper
Hellenic context in which being is closely correlated with intelligibility: real being
is intelligible being. They mean, then, that God is not a somebody or something who
can be discursively defined or discerned with intuitive precision. It is not that his
intelligibility transcends our limited and fallen human intelligences, but that he has
no intelligible content.” For comparably radical (although not identical) readings of
Plotinus’ “negative theology” (or “negative henology,” if “theology” is assumed to
posit God as in any sense a supreme being), see Reiner Schürmann, “L’hénologie
comme dépassement de la métaphysique,” Etudes philosophiques 37 (1982): 331–50;
Jean-Marc Narbonne, “Heidegger et le néoplatonisme,” Quaestio 1 (2001): 55–82;
and idem, Hénologie, ontologie, et Ereignis (Plotin—Proclus—Heidegger) (Paris: Les Belles
Lettres, 2001).

17. See V.3.16.15–16: “There must therefore be a concentration into One,

≠stinoso£n].” Contrast Gerson, Plotinus, 16, and Rist, Plotinus, 25: “Why does
Plotinus generally call it ‘the One’? Surely because it is exactly what it is, an entirely
indivisible unity.”

18. Vladimir Lossky, The Mystical Theology of the Eastern Church (Crestwood,
NY: St. Vladimir’s Seminary Press, 1976), 31, attempting to contrast the One of
Plotinus with the God of Dionysius, fails to note this: “In his refusal to attribute to
God the properties which make up the matter of affirmative theology, Dionysius is
aiming expressly at the neo-platonist definitions: ‘He is neither One, nor Unity.’ ” In
fact, in negating the name ‘One’ Dionysius is simply following the precepts of Plotinus.

culmination.

1. For general treatments of this “motion” see Stephen Gersh, K√nhsiV

2. This does not mean, of course, that Aristotle’s doctrine is identical with
Plato’s. “The good,” for Aristotle, is not a universal: each thing has its own distinct
goodness. See esp. Nicomachean Ethics I.6, 1096a11ff.

3. For form in general as good, see Physics I.9, 192a17.
4. Jonathan Lear, Aristotle: The Desire to Understand (Cambridge: Cambridge

University Press, 1988), 295–96, argues that for Aristotle “the desire which God
inspires is none other than the desire of each organism to realize its form . . . [I]n trying to
realize its form, the organism is doing all that it can do to become intelligible. It is

also doing the best job it can do to imitate God’s thought—and thus to imitate God
himself.” (italics in original). The realized form in a thing is thus the result of its
desire for God.

5. Armstrong in his note ad loc. rightly remarks, “This is one of the funda-
mental principles of Greek philosophical thought.”

6. On this duality of expression see Gersh, From Iamblichus to Eriugena, 48 n.
102.

prop. 31. We saw there that, given the metaphysical ™rc–, we can argue to the
ethical t°loV: here we argue from the ethical t°loV to the metaphysical ™rc–. Both
arguments depend ultimately on the identity of the efficient with the final cause.”
This identity in turn depends on the identity of both with the formal cause.

2. The divine procession Wisdom includes all modes of cognition, from angelic

129NOTES TO CHAPTER FIVE

4. See Dodds, Elements of Theology, 232.
5. Strictly speaking, for Proclus, only the henads are gods, while terms such

as Being, Life, and Intellect are lesser causes which derive their being and causal
power from their proper henads. But Proclus also uses the term ‘god’ more loosely, to
characterize various terms subordinate to the henads. See Dodds, Elements of Theol-
ogy, 261.

6. On the supposed difference between Proclus and Dionysius on this point,
see e.g. Roques, L’univers dionysien, 78–81, esp. 78 n. 3; Ivanka, Plato Christianus,
260–61; Gersh, From Iamblichus to Eriugena, 154ff.

7. See 121, n. 11.
8. Thus Dodds, Elements of Theology, 260, observes that “the principle of con-

tinuity in the vertical procession involved the splitting of each god into a series of
gradually weakening forces, so that Zeus, for example, appears as five different gods each
of whom symbolizes the ‘jovial’ principle on a different plane of reality.” By the same
token, we may say that every god “symbolizes” the One on a different plane of reality.

9. In view of the principle of continuity in Proclus’ “series” (El. Th., prop.
145), this is true not only of the henads themselves but of all the lower levels of
divinity derived from them as well.

10. The real meaning of Proclus’ many divinities is well expressed in Plotinus’
reply to the Gnostics’ denial of such “gods”: “It is not contracting the divine into one
but showing it in that multiplicity in which God himself has shown it, which is
proper to those who know the power of God, inasmuch as, abiding who he is, he
makes many gods, all depending upon himself and existing through him and from
him. And this universe exists through him and looks to him, the whole of it and each
and every one of the gods in it, and it reveals what is his to men” (II.9.9.36–42).
Need Christians quarrel with this? It should rather be directed against the narrow
“monotheism” which regards the world as by nature “profane” rather than as theophany
and God as a unitary being rather than as “all things in all things and nothing in
any.” Just as the God of Dionysius and the Neoplatonists transcends the opposition
between theism and atheism, so he transcends that between monotheism and poly-
theism, for he is strictly speaking neither one nor many in any positive sense.

【15†(S U N Y Series in Ancient Greek Philosophy) Eric David Perl - Theophany_ The Neoplatonic Philosophy of Dionysius the Areopagite (2007).pdf†file-Hkx1s3zBZGQpGFeaY8dqkn】


113CONCLUSION

tween Dionysius and recent phenomenological thought, overcoming the
characteristically modern duality between subject and object. More specifically,
Dionysius’ philosophy can converse fruitfully with such Heideggerian themes
as the critique of “onto-theology” and of “metaphysics of presence” and the
dialectic of revealing and concealing. Heidegger shows how “metaphysics of
presence,” which on his view dominates the whole of Western thought, leads
to a technological comportment toward being in which being is revealed
only as Bestand, resources or stockpile, and how this technological
instrumentalization of being makes possible modern science and leads inevi-
tably to nihilism. As the alternative to this ultimately nihilistic, scientific-
technological stance toward being, Dionysius offers a contemplative-liturgical
stance in which being is not an object for mastery and exploitation but a gift
which is received only in being given and a symbol which is known only in
being unknown. His thought can thus also contribute to contemporary
reflection on technology and the related area of “environmental philosophy.”
The Dionysian, Neoplatonic understanding of all things, including animals,
plants, and minerals, as theophanies which analogously think, live, and love,
may be set against the objectifying and reductionist view of nature which has
led to its exploitation and destruction, and which is largely the result of a
very different religious philosophy that opposes the natural both to the human
and to the divine. In opposition to such a view, Dionysius and his Neoplatonic
predecessors offer a compelling and philosophically grounded vision of all
reality as the presence and manifestation of transcendent divinity.

INTRODUCTION

1. I omit the prefix “pseudo-” on account of its verbal awkwardness and
potentially pejorative connotations.

2. If I have disregarded Dionysius’ background in earlier Christian thought,
which has been recently and extensively investigated by Alexander Golitzin, Et
Introibo ad Altare Dei: The Mystagogy of Dionysius Areopagita, with Special Reference to
Its Predecessors in the Eastern Christian Tradition (Thessalonica: Patriarchikon Idryma
Paterikon Meleton, 1994), this is not because it is unreal or unimportant, but because
it does not contribute to the specifically philosophical understanding of Dionysius.

3. For Proclus’ thought I have relied chiefly on the Elements of Theology
because this work most clearly and systematically presents the fundamental structural
principles of his metaphysics, and it is precisely these principles, rather than the
detailed elaboration of his philosophy in the Platonic Theology and the commentaries,
that contribute most to a philosophical understanding of Dionysius.

【16†(S U N Y Series in Ancient Greek Philosophy) Eric David Perl - Theophany_ The Neoplatonic Philosophy of Dionysius the Areopagite (2007).pdf†file-Hkx1s3zBZGQpGFeaY8dqkn】


25. As Schäfer, Philosophy, 67 n. 26, points out, it is not in Dionysius but only
in Eriugena that the term theophany (qeofºneia, theophania) becomes centrally impor-
tant. Nonetheless, as Schäfer continues, “it quite neatly expresses what is meant here.”

123NOTES TO CHAPTER THREE

26. Cf. 25 and 121 n. 13.
27. Cf. Louis Dupré, The Other Dimension (New York: Doubleday, 1972), 129–

30: “If the infinite is opposed to the finite, it is limited and thereby ceases to be
infinite. Instead we must show that the finite is in the infinite. Which means that our
initial affirmation of the finite must be followed by a negation of the finite’s indepen-
dent being. Such a negation cannot be provided by the argument which asserts the
infinite as also existing and thereby juxtaposes it to the finite . . . True infinity, there-
fore, preserves the finite within itself.”

28. Cf. Plotinus’ statement, quoted earlier, 26, that the One “has no other-
ness.” God as “not other” is of course the theme of Nicholas of Cusa’s treatise De non
aliud, which is strongly influenced by Dionysius.

29. Dodds, Elements of Theology, 217, commenting on Proclus’ doctrine that
the effect is contained, or remains, in the cause and that its going forth depends on
a point of view, remarks, “If this be pressed, it must mean that the separateness of the
lower is an illusion resulting from a partial point of view, and it follows that the
sensible and the intelligible cosmos are both of them appearance, and only the One
fully real.” He adds, “This doctrine was never accepted by the Neoplatonists, but they
often seem to be on the verge of falling into it.” This comment exemplifies the failure
to distinguish between illusion and appearance, and hence a failure to enter into the
very heart of the Neoplatonists’ thought about reality. It is true that for them “the
sensible and intelligible cosmos are both of them appearance,” but it does not follow
that they are “an illusion.”

30. Cf. Balthasar, “Denys,” 164: In Dionysius we find “[m]anifestation con-

The work of Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite stands at a cusp in the history
of thought: it is at once Hellenic and Christian, classical and medieval, philo-
sophical and theological. Unlike the predominantly theological or text-histori-
cal studies which constitute much of the scholarly literature on Dionysius,
Theophany is completely philosophical in nature, placing Dionysius within
the tradition of ancient Greek philosophy and emphasizing, in a positive
light, his continuity with the non-Christian Neoplatonism of Plotinus and
Proclus. Eric D. Perl offers clear expositions of the reasoning that underlies
Neoplatonic philosophy and explains the argumentation that leads to and sup-
ports Neoplatonic doctrines. He includes extensive accounts of fundamental
ideas in Plotinus and Proclus, as well as Dionysius himself, and provides an
excellent philosophical defense of Neoplatonism in general.

“This is, in many ways, the book for which teachers in the fi eld have been wait-
ing: a book that clearly and fully sets out the philosophical logic in Dionysius
in a way accessible to undergraduate students and yet tackles the most vexed
and controverted questions so strongly as to make it a necessity for gradu-
ate students and scholars in the fi eld. Eric Perl has produced a remarkable
unifi cation of philosophy and accurate historical scholarship, something very
rare.” — Wayne J. Hankey, author of One Hundred Years of Neoplatonism in
France: A Brief Philosophical History

Assistant

John Scotus Eriugena's Latin translation of Mystical Theology (Μυστική Θεολογία) by Pseudo-Dionysius the Areopagite is a fascinating case study in the transmission of Neoplatonic metaphysics into the Latin West. His rendering of the Greek text into Latin reflects both his deep engagement with Neoplatonism and his efforts to integrate Dionysian thought into a Carolingian intellectual framework.

1. Translation as Interpretation: Key Choices by Eriugena


Eriugena was not merely translating words but transforming concepts into a Latin metaphysical idiom that was at times quite distinct from the Greek.

a. "Superessentialis" for "ὑπερούσιος" (Hyperousios)


Eriugena’s translation of ὑπερούσιος (hyperousios) as superessentialis is crucial. The Greek term means "beyond being" or "supra-ontological," a fundamental term in Dionysian and Proclean metaphysics. The super- prefix in Latin attempts to capture the Greek ὑπερ- (hyper-), denoting transcendence over being itself. However, while ὑπερούσιος retains a more active and processual sense in Greek, superessentialis risks implying a kind of static state of existence that is merely "above being" rather than dynamically "beyond it."

This reflects Eriugena’s own metaphysical commitments: he saw divine reality as an ontological hierarchy, but one in which being itself could be understood in Neoplatonic terms as a dynamic unfolding (processio) and return (reditus), rather than as something entirely negated.

b. "Superdeus" for "ὑπέρθεος" (Hypertheos)


Eriugena's coinage of superdeus for ὑπέρθεος (Hypertheos, "beyond God") is another fascinating translation choice. Greek ὑπέρθεος conveys a paradox: God is both "God" and "beyond the gods" in a way that destabilizes traditional theological categories. Superdeus retains this paradox but shifts it slightly toward a hierarchical framework in which "God" is explicitly seen as above other divine beings, rather than as beyond all divine predication.

This aligns with Eriugena’s broader system in which God is both absolute transcendence and immediate immanence, a paradox he resolves in his Periphyseon by arguing that God is both nihil (nothing) and omnia (everything).

c. "Caligo" for "Γνόφος" (Gnophos, "Darkness")


Eriugena’s rendering of gnophos as caligo instead of tenebrae is a striking choice. In the Greek, gnophos (e.g., from Exodus 20:21, describing Moses entering "the divine darkness") is a technical term in apophatic theology. It represents not simply an absence of light but a kind of super-bright darkness, a paradoxical presence beyond human perception.

Caligo in Latin, while retaining the sense of obscurity, can also suggest confusion or ignorance. This might be a potential shift in nuance—whereas Dionysius' gnophos signifies a divine unknowability filled with presence, caligo in Latin might suggest something more like the obscurity of fallen human knowledge. However, in later medieval mystical theology, particularly in Eckhart and the Rhineland mystics, caligo was taken up in precisely the Dionysian sense Eriugena was likely intending.

2. Eriugena’s Neoplatonism and the Structure of Reality


Eriugena was deeply influenced by Proclus and the broader Neoplatonic tradition, and his translation choices reflect this. As Eric Perl notes, Dionysius' theology depends on the Neoplatonic triad of monē (remaining), proodos (procession), and epistrophē (return), in which all things emerge from the One and ultimately return to it【18:6†source】.

Eriugena’s Latin, while often literal, tends to reinforce a metaphysical reading in which Dionysian apophasis (negation) is systematically aligned with Proclean return to the divine. This is particularly visible in how he handles Dionysius’ discussions of negation:

  • His use of ablatio (removal) and negatio (negation) as translations of ἀφαίρεσις (aphaeresis) and ἀπόφασις (apophasis) emphasizes the structural logic of negative theology rather than its existential or experiential dimension.

  • Eriugena often adds clarifications that make explicit a Proclean henological (unity-focused) metaphysics rather than a purely Dionysian theology of mystical ascent.


3. The Influence of Eriugena’s Translation


Eriugena’s translation of Mystical Theology was not a neutral scholarly exercise; it shaped how Dionysius was received in the Latin Middle Ages. By providing Latin readers with Neoplatonic categories rendered in Latin scholastic terminology, he helped prepare the way for figures like Albertus Magnus, Thomas Aquinas, and Meister Eckhart to engage with Dionysian thought.

However, because Eriugena’s translation subtly shifts the Dionysian vision toward a more systematic, hierarchical ontology, later medieval readers often interpreted Dionysius through a more rigid Neoplatonic framework than was perhaps present in the original Greek.

4. Conclusion: Eriugena as a Creative Translator


Eriugena’s translation is not simply a passive Latinization but an active philosophical intervention. His choices of superessentialis, superdeus, and caligo reveal his deep commitment to integrating Dionysian thought into a framework that could be assimilated by Carolingian and later Scholastic thinkers. At the same time, his approach risks shifting the experiential, paradoxical, and mystical aspects of Dionysius into a more formalized and structured ontological system.

This tension—between a Dionysian apophatic theology that resists conceptualization and an Eriugenian Neoplatonism that seeks to systematize it—would become a defining feature of later medieval thought on mysticism and negative theology.

INTRODUCTION

This book is the fruit of more than twelve years’ study and teaching of the
thought of Dionysius the Areopagite,1 together with that of Plotinus and
Proclus, as philosophy: not, primarily, as a late antique cultural phenomenon;
nor as an influential episode in the history of Christian theology; nor as
“mysticism,” if that be taken to mean something other than philosophy; nor
as a series of texts with ascertainable relations of influence and citation; but
as philosophy, i.e. as a rationally justified, coherent account of the nature of
reality. Such a philosophical exposition of any body of thought demands
more than an explanation of what the philosopher says and of the sources
from which he derives his doctrines. It requires, above all, an account of the
argumentation, the sequence of reasoning that supports and leads to his
positions. Only by understanding this argumentation can we truly grasp the
meaning of the positions themselves.

In the case of Dionysius, such an understanding is particularly difficult
to achieve because he notoriously eschews argumentation in favor of
proclamatory exposition. (See Ep. VII.1, 1077B–1080A.) But that does not
mean that his thought is not open to philosophical interpretation and pre-
sentation. It simply means that we must look for its underlying argumenta-
tion elsewhere, in the philosophical tradition from which his thought derives.
To take a prime example, the central Dionysian doctrine that God is “be-
yond being” is not merely a phrase or a theme which has a discoverable
history in Plato and Neoplatonism, nor is it merely a vague assertion of
divine transcendence. Rather, within the Neoplatonic context, it is the
conclusion of a definite sequence of philosophical reasoning, and only in
terms of that argumentation can its precise meaning be correctly grasped.
The same is true of other characteristic Dionysian themes such as procession
and reversion, evil as privation, hierarchy, mystical union, and symbolism.
The textual “source” of a given idea in Dionysius may be Proclus, or Plotinus,
or some other writer, pagan or Christian; but its real philosophical origin is
a certain line of reasoning, and this is what I aim to bring to light.

The purpose of this study, therefore, is not to contribute to the extensive
Quellenforschung that has already been undertaken on Dionysius, but rather to
elucidate the meaning and grounds of his vision of reality by looking back
through the philosophical tradition to recover the structures and argumentation

I, and also notes its sources: Dionysius, “Gregory the theologian” (Nazianzus), and
Maximus’ Ambigua. 9

Aristotle’s Categories, Place, and ‘God’
Much of Periphyseon’s first Book analyzes Aristotle’s categories in terms of
their (in)applicability to God and the created universe. Like his contemporaries,
Eriugena knew Aristotle’s treatise only through its Latin paraphrase, the Decem
categoriae, and Porphyry’s Isagoge in Boethius’ translation and commentaries. 10

Praising Aristotle as “the shrewdest among the Greeks . . . in discovering the way
of distinguishing natural things,” John says that he included all created things
“in ten universal genera, which he called the categories, that is, predicables.”11

Yet the categories have their limits, since Augustine writes that they fail utterly
in theology, “the study of the divine essence.” 12 Following both Porphyry’s Pla-
tonizing lead and Augustine’s caution, Eriugena uses the categories to develop
Periphyseon’s themes of divine transcendence and immanence through nature’s
divisions. The result is the most elaborate revision and expansion of the catego-
ries of the early Middle Ages. In the manuscript’s margins, Cusanus’ traces this
development closely. He notes texts on specific categories and terms – ousia or
essence, quantity, body, form, matter, the elements, etc. – and repeats basic points:
“vsia solo intellectu cernitur” (58v, 495C), and all the categories are interrelated
(concathenate, 28v, 472D).

To illustrate John’s analysis of the categories and Cusanus’ glosses, let us look
briefly at Periphyseon’s distinctive account of place. Place defines all finite crea-
tures. As the Student says, “Place is simply the natural definition of each creature,
within which it is wholly contained and beyond which it by no means extends; and
from this it is given to understand that whether one calls it place or limit or term
or definition or circumscription, one and the same thing is denoted, namely, the
confine (ambitus) of a finite creature.”13 The Teacher states the correlation even
more directly, saying that “place is definition and definition is place” – which
Nicholas repeats in the margin (48v, 485B). Here place becomes not simply a mat-

NOTE ON TRANSLATIONS

There is still no adequate English translation of the Dionysian corpus. The
best, that of John Parker,1 is both highly imperfect and largely unavailable;
while the most recent and widely available, that of Colm Luibheid,2 is so far
from the Greek as to be almost a paraphrase rather than a translation, and
disregards Dionysius’ use of traditional philosophical terms. Hence I have
provided my own translations of passages cited from Dionysius, making them
as literal as possible without egregiously violating English usage.

For Plotinus, I have used the translation by A. H. Armstrong in the
Loeb Classical Library,3 with my own modifications where I have judged
these to be necessary for the sake of clarity or precision; and for Proclus’
Elements of Theology, I have used the translation by E. R. Dodds,4 also with
modifications. All other published translations are cited in individual notes.

INTRODUCTION

This book is the fruit of more than twelve years’ study and teaching of the
thought of Dionysius the Areopagite,1 together with that of Plotinus and
Proclus, as philosophy: not, primarily, as a late antique cultural phenomenon;
nor as an influential episode in the history of Christian theology; nor as
“mysticism,” if that be taken to mean something other than philosophy; nor
as a series of texts with ascertainable relations of influence and citation; but
as philosophy, i.e. as a rationally justified, coherent account of the nature of
reality. Such a philosophical exposition of any body of thought demands
more than an explanation of what the philosopher says and of the sources
from which he derives his doctrines. It requires, above all, an account of the
argumentation, the sequence of reasoning that supports and leads to his
positions. Only by understanding this argumentation can we truly grasp the
meaning of the positions themselves.

【4†Donald F. Duclow - Engaging Eriugena, Eckhart and Cusanus (Variorum Collected Studies)-Routledge (2023).pdf†file-935cEdiuCkw9voVqrzr9Db】


omnia explicative”; trans. Hopkins, vol. I, p. 482. See also Apologia, h II, n.28.

32








C O I N C I D I N G I N T H E M A R G I N S

all their multiplicity and contrasts. Moreover, this unfolding marks a theophany, as
the divine nature shines forth in all created things. With Eriugena and Dionysius,
Nicholas thus affirms that “God is the essence of all things” in a twofold sense: as
their transcendent, enfolding source, and as manifest in their unfolding diversity
and multiplicity.

Placing Eriugena within Cusanus’ Quest
By tracing some of Cusanus’ glosses in the British Library’s manuscript, we
have found him to be a shrewd, sympathetic and careful reader of Periphyseon’s
first Book. Along with Nicholas’ other books that survive in Kues, Brussels and
London, the manuscript and its glosses testify to his long career as a voracious
book collector and reader. For Nicholas was a man of many books, whose manu-
scripts, correspondence, speculative works and sermons tell us a great deal about
what he read, and when and how he read it. Here the very extent and variety of
evidence raise a final question for us: What place does Periphyseon occupy within
Cusanus’ wide-ranging reading and writing?

He cites Eriugena by name only three times, unlike his frequent references
to Dionysius, Proclus and others. Yet one citation in the Apologia doctae igno-
rantiae may clarify Eriugena’s role among Nicholas’ sources. For he places
Periphyseon in interesting company alongside not only Dionysius’ writings, but
also Marius Victorinus’ Ad Candidum Arrianum, the Clavis Physicae, David of
Dinant’s books, and Berthold of Moosburg’s commentary on Proclus’ Elements
of Theology. 45 Nicholas highlights the issues of reading and judgment that these
difficult texts raise. Recognizing that they are easily misunderstood, he pro-
poses light censorship, and recommends that they be withheld from “those with
weak mental eyes” who, failing to understand “views to which they are unac-
customed,” condemn their authors as “ignorant and erring.”46 Such misread-
ing had indeed been the fate of Periphyseon, which Pope Honorius III in 1225
condemned as “swarming with worms of heretical perversity”47 – a judgment
which I suspect few of us today would share. And since the Apologia replies to
the Heidelberg theologian Johannes Wenck’s indictment of De docta ignorantia
for heresy, Nicholas may have wished that he had concealed it too from weak-
eyed critics.

The Apologia’s list includes a chain of readers and authors whose works form
a major strand within the medieval Christian Neoplatonic tradition. For Eriuge-
na’s Periphyseon recycled and reshaped the entire Dionysian corpus into a new

【5†(S U N Y Series in Ancient Greek Philosophy) Eric David Perl - Theophany_ The Neoplatonic Philosophy of Dionysius the Areopagite (2007).pdf†file-Hkx1s3zBZGQpGFeaY8dqkn】


25. See above, 23–24.
26. Dionysius’ doctrine of hierarchy could thus be read as an elaboration of

Matthew 20:25–27: “ ‘You know that the rulers of the Gentiles lord it over them, and
their great men exercise authority over them. It shall not be so among you; but
whoever would be great among you must be your servant, and whoever would be first
among you must be your slave.’ ” The higher any being is in the order of reality, the
more it is in the service of—in Dionysius’ terms, providentially proceeds to—all that
is below it. Cf. the contrast of Dionysian hierarchy to the “vulgar” or “political”
model of hierarchy by Marion, The Idol and Distance, 163–64, 170.

27. More clearly than any other interpreter of Dionysius, Rutledge, Cosmic
Theology, 14 n. 1, sees how this overcomes the conventional opposition between
direct and mediated production: “If the whole being and activity of each member is
received it seems to matter little whether we say he or God creates the one imme-
diately below. The immanence and transcendence of God . . . must be stated with
exactly the same emphasis . . . If we say that God creates each member of the hierar-
chy immediately, then . . . we must add immediately that each member is God, at
exactly this level of manifestation or creation” (italics in original).

28. Cf. Louis Bouyer, Cosmos (Petersham, MA: St. Bede’s, 1988), 200.
29. Dionysius’ doctrine of hierarchy thus constitutes an exception to Trouillard’s

claim, “Procession,” 12: “Too often Christian thought about the universe goes from
the parts to the whole and not from the whole to the parts . . . It declares God the
author of things visible and invisible, and not of the visibles by the mediation of the
invisibles . . . Except for Eriugena, we do not find a Christian doctor who overcomes
this lack and here rejoins Neoplatonism.” Such an exception should be no surprise
in Dionysius, the student of Proclus and principal source of Eriugena.

30. The full connotations of this word cannot be captured in a single trans-
lation: “given into their hands,” and in that sense entrusted to them (sc. to be passed
on to others); “lent,” and so not primally theirs, but God-in-them; and “shown” or
“manifested” in them. See A Greek-English Lexicon, ed. Henry George Liddell and

【6†(S U N Y Series in Ancient Greek Philosophy) Eric David Perl - Theophany_ The Neoplatonic Philosophy of Dionysius the Areopagite (2007).pdf†file-Hkx1s3zBZGQpGFeaY8dqkn】


Chapters 1 and 2 correspond in subject-matter to Divine Names I, dis-
cussing, in chapter 1, the radical transcendence and unknowability of God,
and, in chapter 2, the immanence and manifestation of God in all things.

Chapter 3 corresponds to Divine Names IV.1–17, presenting this doc-
trine in its dynamic aspect by discussing Dionysius’ account of God as
Goodness, Beauty, and Love in terms of the cycle of remaining, procession,
and reversion.

Chapter 4 corresponds to Divine Names IV.18–35, addressing the prob-
lem of evil as it arises from such an understanding of reality.

Chapter 5 corresponds to Divine Names V.1–3, discussing the hierar-
chical structuring of being in relation to the doctrine of being as theophany.

Chapter 6 corresponds to Divine Names VII, discussing the nature and
modes of cognition within such an understanding of being.

Chapter 7, finally, presents Dionysius’ philosophy of symbolism as it
emerges from this metaphysics and gnoseology, and is linked primarily with
Celestial Hierarchy I–II.

4 INTRODUCTION

In a manner that perhaps deliberately parallels his own doctrine of
divine names, the author of the Dionysian corpus remains invisible: he lies
hidden behind his works and can be known only as he is manifest in them,
so that the very name Dionysius inevitably refers to the content of the works
rather than to the author.12 The absence of biographical information about
the author encourages a reading of the works in purely philosophical terms,
simply as a body of thought. At the same time, perhaps in part because of his
pseudonymity, Dionysius has tended to be studied not sine but cum ira et studio,
and few expositions of his thought even make a pretense of neutrality.13 The
present study is no exception: my own love for Dionysius will be patent through-
out. But this love is accompanied by, or rather is one with, an equally great
love for Plato and Plotinus, and above all for divine philosophy itself.

CHAPTER ONE

BEYOND BEING AND INTELLIGIBILITY

In recent decades there has been a surge of interest in “negative theology,”1

of which Dionysius is a leading exponent, and hence many studies of this
feature of Dionysius’ thought.2 Rarely, however, do such studies attempt to
present the philosophical argumentation that underlies his teachings. The
doctrine that God or the One, the first principle of reality, lies beyond being
and beyond thought, for Dionysius and his Neoplatonic forebears, is not an
ungrounded starting point or an article of faith but rather the conclusion of
a rigorous sequence of philosophical reasoning, and only by following this
argumentation can we truly understand the doctrine’s meaning. Neoplatonic
and Dionysian “negative theology” and “mysticism” is an aspect of rational
metaphysics, and must be interpreted and evaluated as such. The aim of the
present chapter, therefore, is to expose the philosophical grounds and mean-
ing of Dionysius’ negative theology by showing how the argument behind it
is developed in the Greek philosophical tradition that Dionysius draws on
and continues.
