C U S A N U S ’ S P H I L O S O P H I C A L T E S TA M E N T

Proclus – who cites Origen – comes later. In the passage cited above, Nicholas
then makes the stronger historical claims that Dionysius “imitates” Plato and that
Proclus “follows (sequendo)” Dionysius’s teaching. On this view, the Areopagite
becomes a key intermediary between Plato and Proclus. We now know that Cusa-
nus gets this chronology wrong, and with it his chain of readers and influence.
Dionysius – now Pseudo-Dionysius – relies on Proclus, not the other way around.

【9†(S U N Y Series in Ancient Greek Philosophy) Eric David Perl - Theophany_ The Neoplatonic Philosophy of Dionysius the Areopagite (2007).pdf†file-Hkx1s3zBZGQpGFeaY8dqkn】


113CONCLUSION

tween Dionysius and recent phenomenological thought, overcoming the
characteristically modern duality between subject and object. More specifically,
Dionysius’ philosophy can converse fruitfully with such Heideggerian themes
as the critique of “onto-theology” and of “metaphysics of presence” and the
dialectic of revealing and concealing. Heidegger shows how “metaphysics of
presence,” which on his view dominates the whole of Western thought, leads
to a technological comportment toward being in which being is revealed
only as Bestand, resources or stockpile, and how this technological
instrumentalization of being makes possible modern science and leads inevi-
tably to nihilism. As the alternative to this ultimately nihilistic, scientific-
technological stance toward being, Dionysius offers a contemplative-liturgical
stance in which being is not an object for mastery and exploitation but a gift
which is received only in being given and a symbol which is known only in
being unknown. His thought can thus also contribute to contemporary
reflection on technology and the related area of “environmental philosophy.”
The Dionysian, Neoplatonic understanding of all things, including animals,
plants, and minerals, as theophanies which analogously think, live, and love,
may be set against the objectifying and reductionist view of nature which has
led to its exploitation and destruction, and which is largely the result of a
very different religious philosophy that opposes the natural both to the human
and to the divine. In opposition to such a view, Dionysius and his Neoplatonic
predecessors offer a compelling and philosophically grounded vision of all
reality as the presence and manifestation of transcendent divinity.

INTRODUCTION

1. I omit the prefix “pseudo-” on account of its verbal awkwardness and
potentially pejorative connotations.

2. If I have disregarded Dionysius’ background in earlier Christian thought,
which has been recently and extensively investigated by Alexander Golitzin, Et
Introibo ad Altare Dei: The Mystagogy of Dionysius Areopagita, with Special Reference to

it does not contribute to the specifically philosophical understanding of Dionysius.

3. For Proclus’ thought I have relied chiefly on the Elements of Theology
because this work most clearly and systematically presents the fundamental structural
principles of his metaphysics, and it is precisely these principles, rather than the
detailed elaboration of his philosophy in the Platonic Theology and the commentaries,
that contribute most to a philosophical understanding of Dionysius.

【10†(S U N Y Series in Ancient Greek Philosophy) Eric David Perl - Theophany_ The Neoplatonic Philosophy of Dionysius the Areopagite (2007).pdf†file-Hkx1s3zBZGQpGFeaY8dqkn】


23. Dionysius’ view of evil as deficiency of being and hence as uncaused and
unintelligible is thus closely comparable to Augustine’s, although Augustine thinks
more of efficient causality and Dionysius more of final causality. Having traced the
origin of evil back as far as the will of a rational creature, Augustine then says, “Let
no one, then, seek an efficient cause of an evil will. For its cause is not efficient, but
deficient, because the evil will itself is not an effect of something, but a defect . . . Now
to seek the causes of these defections, which are . . . not efficient causes, but deficient,
is like wishing to see darkness or hear silence . . . Thus, too, our mind perceives
intelligible forms by understanding them; but when they are deficient, it knows them
by not knowing them; for ‘who can understand his failings?’ . . . [T]he will is made
evil by nothing else than the defection by which God is forsaken: a defection of
which the cause, too, is certainly deficient.” Augustine, The City of God against the
Pagans, ed. and tr. R. W. Dyson (Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, 1998),
XII.7–9, 507–09.

Aristotle’s Categories, Place, and ‘God’
Much of Periphyseon’s first Book analyzes Aristotle’s categories in terms of
their (in)applicability to God and the created universe. Like his contemporaries,
Eriugena knew Aristotle’s treatise only through its Latin paraphrase, the Decem
categoriae, and Porphyry’s Isagoge in Boethius’ translation and commentaries. 10

Praising Aristotle as “the shrewdest among the Greeks . . . in discovering the way
of distinguishing natural things,” John says that he included all created things
“in ten universal genera, which he called the categories, that is, predicables.”11

Yet the categories have their limits, since Augustine writes that they fail utterly
in theology, “the study of the divine essence.” 12 Following both Porphyry’s Pla-
tonizing lead and Augustine’s caution, Eriugena uses the categories to develop
Periphyseon’s themes of divine transcendence and immanence through nature’s
divisions. The result is the most elaborate revision and expansion of the catego-
ries of the early Middle Ages. In the manuscript’s margins, Cusanus’ traces this
development closely. He notes texts on specific categories and terms – ousia or
essence, quantity, body, form, matter, the elements, etc. – and repeats basic points:
“vsia solo intellectu cernitur” (58v, 495C), and all the categories are interrelated
(concathenate, 28v, 472D).

In light of this passage, we cannot ignore the ghost in the room – Dionysius the
Areopagite. As John Monfasani has shown, Nicholas knew the suspicions con-
cerning the dating and authorship of the Dionysian corpus that emerged in Rome
beginning in the 1450s.51 Lorenzo Valla challenged the works’ authenticity, but
made no mention of their similarity to Proclus. Yet in Cusanus’s dialogue De non
aliud (1462), Pietro Balbi – who was translating the Theology of Plato – asks
the Cardinal to explain precisely these similarities. He replies, “It is certain that
your Proclus was later in time than Dionysius the Areopagite. But it is uncer-
tain whether he saw the writings of Dionysius.”52 Here Nicholas leaves open the
question of his influence on Proclus. Balbi and the Cardinal then discuss the two
thinkers’ similar statements placing the “existing one” after “the unqualifiedly
One,” and note Proclus’s reliance on Plato for this view. A year later in De vena-
tione sapientiae, Nicholas reaffirms Dionysius’s apostolic dating, and states that

20. The question of whether Dionysius’ mysticism is based on “experience” is
irrelevant here, where our purpose is to provide a philosophical account of its mean-
ing. The interpretation of mysticism in terms of “experience” has in any case recently
and rightly been subjected to criticism as distinctively modern. See Michael Sells,
Mystical Languages of Unsaying (Chicago: University of Chicago Press, 1994), 214: “If
the nonintentionality claims of apophatic mystics are taken seriously, and if experi-
ence is, by definition, intentional, it necessarily follows that mystical union is not an
experience. All experience must have a grammatical object, but the prime motivation
of apophatic language is to subvert or displace the grammatical object . . . If it is true
that all experience is constructed, it is equally true that the concept of experience is
a modern construct . . . The apophatic mystic speaks of the birth of the son in the soul,
of annihilation, of an awakening without an awakener, but does not speak of ‘the
experience’ of such birth, annihilation, or awakening.” This is emphatically the case for
Dionysius, with the substitution of “union,” “darkness,” “unknowing,” for “birth,” “an-
nihilation,” etc. See also Golitzin, Et Introibo, 31–32. On this issue with regard to
Plotinus see John Bussanich, “Plotinian Mysticism in Theoretical and Comparative
Perspective,” American Catholic Philosophical Quarterly 71 (1997): 339–65.

Assistant

Expanding on the close reading of Eriugena’s translation choices with Duclow’s insights, we can identify several additional key translation decisions that reflect both his metaphysical commitments and his adaptation of Dionysian Neoplatonism into the Latin intellectual framework.

1. "Superessentialis" and the Problem of Being


One of Eriugena’s most striking translation choices is his frequent use of superessentialis for the Greek ὑπερούσιος. This term, which can be translated as "beyond essence" or "supra-essential," reflects the fundamental Neoplatonic idea that God is beyond being (ὑπὲρ τὴν οὐσίαν). Dionysius' original Greek expresses this paradox through a dialectical play between affirmation and negation, which Eriugena maintains but also amplifies through Latin’s less flexible metaphysical vocabulary.

Duclow discusses how Eriugena extends this negative theology into a systematic philosophical vision, where God is not simply beyond being in a negative sense but is also the ground from which all being emerges【26:1†source】. The use of superessentialis thus serves both as a preservation of the Greek concept and as an intensification of Eriugena’s own metaphysical commitments.

2. "Caligo" as a Translation of "Γνόφος"


Eriugena renders the Greek γνόφος as caligo, meaning "darkness" or "mist." In Dionysius, γνόφος has the connotation of a mystical obscurity, reminiscent of the biblical cloud of unknowing from Exodus. Eriugena’s caligo strongly evokes the idea of an apophatic darkness that is at once an absence and a presence.

Duclow notes that this rendering aligns with Eriugena’s own epistemological framework, where divine knowledge is attained through a process of "excessus"—going beyond sensory and intellectual comprehension【26:1†source】. By choosing caligo, Eriugena strengthens the paradox of a luminous darkness, which later influenced medieval mystics such as Eckhart and Cusanus.

3. "Ablatio" and the Dialectic of Negation


Another crucial translation choice is ablatio, used for ἀφαίρεσις (removal, negation). In Dionysius, ἀφαίρεσις is central to the apophatic method: one strips away all conceptual predicates to approach divine unknowability. Eriugena’s ablatio retains this meaning but also introduces a legal or scholastic nuance, suggesting an active taking-away rather than a simple negation.

Duclow emphasizes how Eriugena’s choice of ablatio reflects his broader metaphysical vision, in which negation is not merely the absence of predicates but an essential process of intellectual purification【26:1†source】. This anticipates later medieval formulations of negative theology, particularly in the Victorine and Cusan traditions.

4. The Ambiguity of "Intellectus" for "Νοῦς"


Eriugena often translates νοῦς as intellectus, which aligns with both Augustinian and Neoplatonic traditions. However, νοῦς in Dionysius is a highly fluid concept, encompassing both the divine intellect and human rationality. By using intellectus, Eriugena introduces a scholastic tone that was not as present in the original Greek.

Duclow notes that this translation subtly shifts the emphasis toward a participatory model of cognition, where the human intellectus must ascend through contemplation to the divine nous【26:1†source】. This reflects Eriugena’s broader speculative metaphysics, in which all reality is structured as an interweaving of being and non-being.

5. "Mystica Theologia" as a Title


Eriugena’s choice to render the title as De Mystica Theologia preserves the original but also adapts it to a Latin readership familiar with Christian mystical traditions. The term mystica in Latin had already developed strong connotations of sacramental theology and monastic contemplation, whereas the Greek μυστικός in Dionysius retains a more philosophical and initiatory nuance.

This translation choice places Dionysius squarely within the Latin mystical tradition, but as Duclow points out, it also helps Eriugena integrate Dionysian thought into his broader system of Periphyseon, where mystical ascent is framed as an ontological return (reditus)【26:1†source】.

Conclusion


Eriugena’s translation is far more than a linguistic exercise; it is a philosophical re-interpretation that subtly reshapes Dionysian thought within a Carolingian intellectual framework. His choices—superessentialis, caligo, ablatio, intellectus, and mystica theologia—reveal his metaphysical commitments and his desire to integrate Neoplatonic and Christian traditions into a coherent vision of divine transcendence and human participation.

How does this excessus relate to the transcendental philosophy of Kant and Hegel

Assistant

The concept of excessus in Eriugena’s mystical theology and Neoplatonism can be fruitfully compared to the transcendental philosophy of Kant and the dialectical idealism of Hegel. While these systems emerge from different historical and intellectual contexts, they share common concerns about the limits of human cognition, the dialectical structure of reality, and the role of negation in philosophical inquiry.

1. Eriugena’s Excessus and the Limits of Cognition


Eriugena’s excessus describes the movement beyond sensory and rational knowledge toward an encounter with the divine through apophasis (negation). This corresponds to his broader ontology, in which God is both the source (principium) and the end (reditus) of all being.

  • Excessus is not merely an epistemic limit but an ontological process: the human intellect moves beyond discursive knowledge into an unknowable darkness (caligo), where God is encountered beyond being (superessentialis).

  • This movement is fundamentally dialectical: knowledge is attained through negation, culminating in a paradox where divine ignorance (docta ignorantia) becomes the highest form of knowing.


This bears a resemblance to Kant’s transcendental limits and Hegel’s dialectical movement, but in different ways.

2. Kant: The Limits of Reason and the Noumenon


Kant’s Critique of Pure Reason (1781) establishes a fundamental division between the phenomenal realm (what can be known through the categories of understanding) and the noumenal realm (what lies beyond possible experience).

  • For Kant, reason has an inherent drive to exceed its own limits, leading to antinomies (self-contradictions).

  • The noumenon (thing-in-itself) is an unknowable beyond, analogous in some ways to Eriugena’s superessentialis, which cannot be positively grasped.

  • However, where Eriugena sees an ontological ascent into divine union, Kant maintains that this limit is absolute: we can think the noumenon, but we cannot know it.


Comparison to Eriugena:
  • Eriugena and Kant agree that human cognition has limits, but Kant denies the possibility of moving beyond them through mystical or dialectical ascent.

  • In contrast, Eriugena sees excessus as an ontological imperative: the intellect is called to go beyond its own conditions toward union with the divine.


3. Hegel: Dialectics and the Overcoming of Finitude


Hegel, in Phenomenology of Spirit (1807) and Science of Logic (1812-16), moves beyond Kant by resolving the problem of transcendence through dialectics. For Hegel, what appears as an absolute limit (like Kant’s noumenon) is actually a moment in the self-unfolding of Absolute Spirit.

  • Negation as a Mode of Knowing: In Hegel’s dialectic, every finite concept negates itself and is sublated (aufgehoben) into a higher unity.

  • The Unfolding of the Absolute: Hegel describes a logical movement from being to nothing to becoming, mirroring the way Eriugena describes God as beyond all categories (nihil and omnia).

  • Human Thought Realizing Itself as Divine: Hegel’s Absolute Spirit ultimately recognizes itself through historical and philosophical development—an idea that echoes Eriugena’s notion that all being returns to God.


Comparison to Eriugena:
  • Eriugena and Hegel both use negation as a means of ascent, but Hegel’s negation is an immanent dialectical process, whereas Eriugena’s excessus is a mystical movement toward divine unknowability.

  • Hegel internalizes transcendence: the Absolute is realized through history and philosophy, whereas Eriugena sees transcendence as an ontological movement beyond reason.


4. Conclusion: Excessus, Dialectics, and the Problem of Transcendence


FeatureEriugena (Excessus)Kant (Transcendental Limits)Hegel (Dialectics of Absolute Spirit)
Nature of the BeyondGod as superessentialis, beyond being and knowingThe noumenon is unknowable and unreachableThe Absolute is realized through dialectical development
Role of NegationApophatic (negative) theology removes all predicates to reach divine unknowabilityReason negates itself when it tries to exceed its limitsNegation is dialectical: every finite concept is sublated into a higher unity
Knowledge and UnionThe mind ascends beyond rationality into mystical union with the divineHuman reason is limited to the phenomenal realmThe dialectical process leads to Absolute Knowing
Metaphysical ImplicationsReality is structured as a return to God (reditus) through excessusKnowledge is bounded by conditions of experienceSpirit realizes itself through negation and self-overcoming

Eriugena’s excessus anticipates Kant’s notion of the limits of cognition, but unlike Kant, he believes these limits can be transcended. Meanwhile, Hegel’s dialectics resonate with Eriugena’s negation, but where Eriugena negates to reach an unknowable God, Hegel negates to achieve self-knowledge.

Thus, Eriugena’s thought can be seen as a pre-modern apophatic dialectic, pointing toward both Kantian epistemology and Hegelian metaphysics, but without collapsing divine transcendence into historical self-realization.

make a glossary of latin terms from the translation by chapter explaining the metaphysical dimensions

Assistant

Glossary of Latin Terms in Eriugena’s Translation of Mystical Theology

This glossary provides an overview of key Latin terms from John Scotus Eriugena’s translation of Mystical Theology, explaining their metaphysical significance in relation to Neoplatonism, apophatic theology, and Eriugena’s own philosophical framework. The terms are organized by chapter.

Chapter I: De mystica theologia


1. Superessentialis (from Greek ὑπερούσιος)


Meaning: “Beyond essence” or “super-essential.”
Metaphysical Significance: This term reflects the Neoplatonic idea that God is beyond being (ἐπέκεινα τῆς οὐσίας in Plotinus). Eriugena’s use of superessentialis aligns with his ontology in Periphyseon, where God is both nihil (nothing) and omnia (everything). The divine reality is not just above being but constitutes the ineffable ground from which all being emerges.

2. Superdeus (from Greek ὑπέρθεος)


Meaning: “Beyond God.”
Metaphysical Significance: A paradoxical term used to emphasize that the divine transcends all concepts of divinity. This aligns with Pseudo-Dionysius’ negative theology: even the name “God” must be negated to approach the unknowable divine reality.

3. Caligo (from Greek γνόφος)


Meaning: “Darkness” or “mystical obscurity.”
Metaphysical Significance: The term conveys the Dionysian idea of the cloud of unknowing, where the highest divine knowledge is attained through ignorance. In Eriugena’s system, this represents the excessus of the mind beyond discursive reasoning into mystical union.

4. Ablatio (from Greek ἀφαίρεσις)


Meaning: “Removal” or “stripping away.”
Metaphysical Significance: Essential to apophatic theology, ablatio is the process of negating finite attributes to reach divine transcendence. In Eriugena, this is also a cognitive process of purification, removing all determinations to attain pure divine presence.

5. Intellectus (from Greek νοῦς)


Meaning: “Intellect” or “higher mind.”
Metaphysical Significance: Eriugena follows the Neoplatonic tradition in viewing intellectus as both human and divine. The intellectus must be stripped of its conceptual constraints through excessus (mystical transcendence) to reach divine truth.

Chapter II: Quomodo oportet et uniri et hymnos referre


6. Mystica theologia (from Greek μυστικὴ θεολογία)


Meaning: “Mystical theology.”
Metaphysical Significance: This term encapsulates the entire method of approaching the divine through negation and ineffability. Mystica in Latin retains its Greek meaning of hidden or initiatory knowledge, linking theology to direct, non-discursive experience.

7. Superlucens


Meaning: “Super-shining” or “beyond light.”
Metaphysical Significance: The paradox of divine darkness (caligo) being also superlucens reflects the Dionysian notion that God is both unknowable and the source of all illumination. In Eriugena’s thought, this connects to the idea that divine transcendence manifests through negation.

8. Silentium


Meaning: “Silence.”
Metaphysical Significance: Silence in Mystical Theology is not mere absence but the highest form of divine communication. Eriugena aligns with Dionysius in seeing silentium as a necessary stage of mystical ascent where speech fails.

Chapter III: Quae sunt καταφατικαί theologiae, quae αποφατικαί


9. Cataphatica theologia (from Greek καταφατικὴ θεολογία)


Meaning: “Affirmative theology.”
Metaphysical Significance: The method of describing God through positive attributes (God is light, life, wisdom). Eriugena acknowledges this approach but subordinates it to negative theology (apophatica theologia), which he considers higher.

10. Apophatica theologia (from Greek ἀποφατικὴ θεολογία)


Meaning: “Negative theology.”
Metaphysical Significance: The negation of all concepts attributed to God. Eriugena extends Dionysian apophasis by incorporating it into his broader metaphysical system, in which all being emanates from God and returns through a process of negation and transcendence.

11. Nomina divina


Meaning: “Divine names.”
Metaphysical Significance: The divine names (sapientia, vita, virtus) are affirmations that are ultimately negated. Eriugena follows Dionysius in arguing that all names applied to God must be transcended to reach divine union.

Chapter IV: Quia nihil sensibilium omnis sensibilis per excellentiam causalis


12. Nihil (from Greek μηδέν)


Meaning: “Nothingness.”
Metaphysical Significance: Eriugena follows the Neoplatonic paradox where God is both nihil (because He transcends being) and omnia (because all things emerge from Him). This anticipates later medieval mysticism, especially Nicholas of Cusa’s docta ignorantia.

13. Sensibilia


Meaning: “Sensible things.”
Metaphysical Significance: Eriugena distinguishes sensibilia from intelligibilia (intelligible things), aligning with the Platonic hierarchy of reality where the sensible world is a shadow of divine truth.

14. Lux


Meaning: “Light.”
Metaphysical Significance: Divine light is both a metaphor for knowledge and something to be negated. In Dionysius and Eriugena, true enlightenment occurs through transcending light into the darkness (caligo).

Chapter V: Quia nihil intelligibilium omnis intelligibilis per excellentiam causalis


15. Intelligibilia


Meaning: “Intelligible things.”
Metaphysical Significance: The realm of intelligibilia is higher than sensibilia but must still be negated in the mystical ascent. Eriugena incorporates this into his hierarchy of nature in Periphyseon, where intellect (intellectus) is still finite compared to God.

16. Unitas


Meaning: “Unity.”
Metaphysical Significance: The divine is the absolute unitas, beyond all multiplicity. Eriugena follows Proclus in seeing the One as prior to being, but he also integrates this into a Christian framework where unity and trinity coexist.

17. Excedere


Meaning: “To transcend” (excessus in noun form).
Metaphysical Significance: The term describes the movement beyond being, knowledge, and sensation toward divine union. Excessus is not just a limit (as in Kant) but an active process leading to divinization (deificatio).

18. Docta ignorantia


Meaning: “Learned ignorance.”
Metaphysical Significance: Though not an explicit term in Eriugena’s translation, this idea pervades his work. It signifies that true knowledge of God comes through recognizing the limits of knowledge, anticipating Nicholas of Cusa’s later use of the term.

Conclusion: The Metaphysical Function of Eriugena’s Latin Terms


Eriugena’s translation choices are deeply metaphysical, reflecting both his fidelity to Dionysius and his own speculative theology. His use of:
  • Superessentialis, nihil, and superdeus affirm his commitment to an apophatic vision of God.

  • Caligo, silentium, and excessus reinforce the mystical ascent beyond rational thought.

  • Ablatio, apophatica theologia, and unitas align with Neoplatonic structures of negation and return.


In sum, Eriugena’s Latin terminology is both a faithful rendering of Dionysian negative theology and an expansion into a systematic Christian Neoplatonism that profoundly shaped medieval thought.

Expanded Glossary of Latin Terms in Eriugena’s Translation of Mystical Theology

This expanded glossary focuses on additional Latin terms from Eriugena’s translation that illustrate notable translation choices and key metaphysical concepts in both Pseudo-Dionysius (D) and John Scottus Eriugena (E). The terms emphasize how Eriugena adapts Greek Neoplatonism into Latin, incorporating his own philosophical commitments.

Chapter I: De mystica theologia

19. Inconversibilia (from Greek ἀνακύκλωτα)


Meaning: “Unchangeable” or “irreversible.”
Metaphysical Significance: In Dionysius, this term refers to divine mysteries that are not subject to change or alteration. Eriugena’s use of inconversibilia emphasizes the idea that the divine realm is beyond mutability, contrasting it with the created order, which exists in cycles of procession (proodos) and return (epistrophē).

20. Verticem (from Greek ἄκρον)


Meaning: “Peak” or “summit.”
Metaphysical Significance: The term conveys the idea of mystical ascent, reflecting both Dionysius' hierarchy of being and Eriugena’s concept of the intellect moving toward its divine source. In Eriugena, the summit of divine knowledge is reached through negation, aligning with Neoplatonic henosis (union with the One).

21. Sublimissimum (from Greek ὑπέρτατον)


Meaning: “Most sublime” or “supremely elevated.”
Metaphysical Significance: This term captures Dionysius' hierarchical structure of reality, where God exists beyond all things. Eriugena’s translation reinforces a vision of transcendence where the highest reality (sublimissimum) is accessed through the negation of all lower realities.

22. Relucet (from Greek ἀναλάμπει)


Meaning: “Shines forth” or “is reflected.”
Metaphysical Significance: In Dionysius, divine light is paradoxically both hidden and manifest. Eriugena’s relucet suggests that all things participate in divine illumination, consistent with his belief that creation is a theophany—a visible manifestation of divine truth.

23. Invisibilia (from Greek ἀόρατα)


Meaning: “Invisible things.”
Metaphysical Significance: For both Dionysius and Eriugena, the invisible is superior to the visible, since true reality is beyond sense perception. This aligns with Neoplatonic epistemology, where the highest knowledge is attained through the intellect’s direct apprehension of the unseen.

Chapter II: Quomodo oportet et uniri et hymnos referre

24. Incircumvelate (from Greek ἀνεπίδετον περιβολῆς)


Meaning: “Unenclosed” or “uncontained.”
Metaphysical Significance: This term expresses divine infinity—the idea that God is not enclosed within any boundary or conceptual framework. Eriugena, like Dionysius, insists that the divine cannot be comprehended by finite minds, emphasizing the necessity of apophatic negation.

25. Absolutus (from Greek ἀπαλλαγμένος)


Meaning: “Freed” or “detached.”
Metaphysical Significance: In Dionysius, this term relates to the idea of detachment from all finite realities in order to attain divine union. Eriugena expands this notion into a metaphysical principle: the divine is absolutely beyond all categories, including being and non-being.

26. Tenebras latibulum (from Greek σκοτεινὸς λήθη)


Meaning: “Darkness as a hiding place.”
Metaphysical Significance: In Dionysius, divine darkness is not mere absence but a hidden plenitude. Eriugena’s Latin reinforces the paradox: divine knowledge is found in a darkness that conceals and reveals simultaneously.

27. Positiones et ablationes (from Greek θέσεις καὶ ἀφαιρέσεις)


Meaning: “Affirmations and removals.”
Metaphysical Significance: This phrase captures the Dionysian dialectic of kataphasis (positive theology) and apophasis (negative theology). Eriugena emphasizes that the divine transcends both affirmation and negation, moving beyond all human conceptualization.

Chapter III: Quae sunt καταφατικαί theologiae, quae αποφατικαί

28. Transnominationes (from Greek μετωνυμίαι)


Meaning: “Transpositions of names.”
Metaphysical Significance: Eriugena recognizes that all divine names (nomina divina) are metaphorical and ultimately inadequate. This reflects both Dionysian and Proclean thought, where divine names must be transcended.

29. Praestantissimis (from Greek ἀκροτάτοις)


Meaning: “Most excellent” or “highest.”
Metaphysical Significance: In Dionysius, the divine names originate from the highest intellectual level of the hierarchy. Eriugena reinforces this hierarchical view of divine predication, reflecting his broader vision of nature as a structured order of descent and return.

Chapter IV: Quia nihil sensibilium omnis sensibilis per excellentiam causalis

30. Impassibilitas (from Greek ἀπάθεια)


Meaning: “Impassibility” or “freedom from passion.”
Metaphysical Significance: In both Dionysius and Eriugena, God is beyond all change and passion (pathos). However, Eriugena integrates this into his doctrine of divine simplicity, in which God is beyond all forms of composition.

31. Commota (from Greek ταραχώδης)


Meaning: “Disturbed” or “agitated.”
Metaphysical Significance: Eriugena insists that the divine is utterly beyond motion or instability, aligning with his Neoplatonic understanding of divine immutability (ἀκίνητον).

32. Partitionem (from Greek διαίρεσις)


Meaning: “Division” or “separation.”
Metaphysical Significance: Eriugena translates διαίρεσις in a way that suggests that divine reality is absolutely undivided, reinforcing the unity of being in Neoplatonic thought.

Chapter V: Quia nihil intelligibilium omnis intelligibilis per excellentiam causalis

33. Similitudo et dissimilitudo (from Greek ὁμοιότης καὶ ἀνομοιότης)


Meaning: “Similarity and dissimilarity.”
Metaphysical Significance: In Dionysius, divine names describe God through both likeness and unlikeness. Eriugena preserves this paradox, indicating that God is at once the most knowable and the most unknowable.

34. Nihil existentium (from Greek μηδὲν τῶν ὄντων)


Meaning: “Nothing of existing things.”
Metaphysical Significance: Eriugena’s translation emphasizes that God is beyond all existing entities, anticipating later Christian Neoplatonic thought (e.g., Eckhart’s Gott ist Nichts).

35. Verbum eius non est (from Greek οὐκ ἔστιν αὐτοῦ λόγος)


Meaning: “His Word is not.”
Metaphysical Significance: This phrase challenges theological realism: for Eriugena, even the divine Word (Logos) is beyond being and non-being.

Conclusion: Eriugena’s Latin as a Metaphysical Framework


  • His Latin preserves and expands Dionysius’ apophatic theology, reinforcing the paradox of divine transcendence.

  • He systematically incorporates Neoplatonic hierarchy, structuring divine knowledge as a movement through negation (ablatio).

  • His choice of philosophically dense Latin terms such as superessentialis, caligo, and nihil existentium reflects a metaphysical synthesis of Dionysius, Augustine, and Proclus.


Through these translation choices, Eriugena’s Mystical Theology becomes not just an interpretation of Dionysius, but a foundational text in medieval Christian Neoplatonism.

So E uses two different latin words for darkness?

Assistant

Yes, Eriugena uses two different Latin words for darkness:

1. Caligo (from Greek γνόφος, gnophos)
2. Tenebrae (from Greek σκότος, skotos)

These choices are not arbitrary but reflect subtle philosophical and theological distinctions in both Pseudo-Dionysius' apophatic theology and Eriugena's own metaphysical framework.

1. Caligo as Mystical Darkness


Greek Source: γνόφος (gnophos)
Eriugena’s Latin: Caligo
Meaning: A divine, luminous darkness

Metaphysical Significance


  • Caligo conveys the paradoxical nature of the divine: it is unknowable yet overflowing with presence.

  • In Dionysius, gnophos is used in reference to Moses ascending into the cloud of divine unknowing (Exodus 20:21). The idea is that God is beyond conceptual knowledge, so the closer one gets to Him, the less one "sees."

  • Eriugena retains this meaning: divine knowledge is attained only through excessus, where the mind transcends itself into a radiant obscurity.


💡 Key Interpretation:
Caligo is not just darkness in a literal sense, but an intellectual or mystical obscurity—a realm beyond being and knowing.

2. Tenebrae as Absence and Privation


Greek Source: σκότος (skotos)
Eriugena’s Latin: Tenebrae
Meaning: Darkness as the absence of light

Metaphysical Significance


  • Tenebrae usually denotes privation—a darkness that results from the absence of divine illumination.

  • In Dionysius, skotos often appears in contrast to phos (light), emphasizing the opposition between ignorance and knowledge.

  • Eriugena uses tenebrae carefully: it often represents the lower, fallen darkness—that which lacks participation in divine light.


💡 Key Interpretation:
Unlike caligo, which signifies the highest form of divine experience, tenebrae is negative—it represents separation from the source of light, akin to Plato’s Cave.

3. Why Does Eriugena Use Two Words for Darkness?


Eriugena’s use of caligo and tenebrae reflects two different types of negation in Dionysian negative theology:

Latin TermGreek TermMeaningMetaphysical Role
CaligoΓνόφος (gnophos)Mystical darknessThe paradoxical fullness of divine unknowability
TenebraeΣκότος (skotos)Darkness as absenceLack of divine illumination, akin to ignorance

By distinguishing these two, Eriugena preserves the Neoplatonic hierarchy:

  • Caligo belongs to theophany—a super-radiant obscurity where the divine is beyond comprehension.

  • Tenebrae belongs to privation—a lower darkness where creatures are separated from divine light.


This distinction is essential to Eriugena’s metaphysics, since he sees all reality as structured hierarchically: creatures move from tenebrae (ignorance) toward caligo (mystical darkness), where they can be reunited with the divine.

4. Influence on Later Mysticism


This dual concept of darkness profoundly influenced later medieval mystics, including:
  • Meister Eckhart: Who speaks of the "Divine Darkness" (caligo as the highest knowledge).

  • Nicholas of Cusa: His docta ignorantia builds on Eriugena’s idea that God is known in the unknown.

  • The Cloud of Unknowing: Which describes mystical ascent as moving beyond reason into divine obscurity.


5. Conclusion: Darkness as the Path to God


Eriugena’s careful distinction between caligo (the luminous obscurity of God) and tenebrae (the absence of divine presence) is a brilliant philosophical adaptation of Dionysius’ Greek into Latin.
This reinforces his broader system where:
  • God is unknowable yet present (caligo).

  • Creatures are in darkness until they return (tenebrae → caligo → divine union).


Thus, for Eriugena, the journey to God is not toward light, but into the highest form of darkness—a mystical negation where knowledge ends and union begins.

expand the glossary (without repeats) to include terms that are key to neoplatonism

Expanded Glossary of Latin Terms in Eriugena’s Translation of Mystical Theology


(Focusing on key Neoplatonic concepts without repeating previously covered terms)

This expanded glossary highlights Neoplatonic metaphysical concepts in Eriugena’s translation, showing how he adapts Dionysius’ thought into a Latin philosophical framework. The terms emphasize hierarchical being, procession and return, divine unity, and apophatic negation.

1. Hierarchical Being and the Divine Order

36. Principium (from Greek ἀρχή, archē)


Meaning: “Principle” or “origin.”
Neoplatonic Significance: Archē in Neoplatonism refers to the ultimate source of reality—the One (τὸ ἕν) or God as the first principle.
  • Eriugena’s use of principium reflects his belief that God is the origin of all things but also beyond origin.

  • In Plotinus, archē is the One, from which all things emanate (proodos).

  • In Dionysius, archē is the hidden source—God is beyond all predicates yet the cause of all.


37. Causalis (from Greek αἴτιος, aitios)


Meaning: “Causal” or “pertaining to cause.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • In Neoplatonism, the One is the universal cause of all reality.

  • Dionysius calls God the causa omnium (“cause of all”), which Eriugena translates to emphasize God’s transcendental causality.

  • Unlike Aristotelian causality, this divine causality is non-dual: God is not just a first mover but a source that overflows into creation.


38. Processio (from Greek πρόοδος, proodos)


Meaning: “Procession” or “emanation.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • Processio describes how all things “flow out” from the divine without diminishing it.

  • Plotinus’ triadic structure:

1. Monē (remaining in the One)
2. Proodos (procession from the One)
3. Epistrophē (return to the One)
  • Dionysius and Eriugena adopt this: creation is a processio from God and a reditus (return) to Him.

  • Christianized Neoplatonism: Procession is not necessary emanation, but divine self-giving (theophany).


39. Reditus (from Greek ἐπιστροφή, epistrophē)


Meaning: “Return.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • In Plotinus, epistrophē is the soul’s journey back to the One after descending into multiplicity.

  • Eriugena’s theology is structured around reditus:

- All things originate in God (principium).
- All things proceed from God (processio).
- All things return to God (reditus).
  • Mystical union is the highest form of reditus.


2. Unity and Multiplicity

40. Unitas (from Greek ἕν, hen)


Meaning: “Oneness” or “unity.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • The One (τὸ ἕν) in Plotinus is beyond being, utterly simple, and the source of all reality.

  • Dionysius and Eriugena preserve this absolute unity as the foundation of negative theology:

- God is not “one” in a numerical sense (because that would limit Him).
- God is beyond unity and plurality.
- The intellect can only approach unity by negating multiplicity.

41. Multiplicitas (from Greek πολλαπλότης, pollaplótēs)


Meaning: “Multiplicity” or “manifoldness.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • In Neoplatonism, the One overflows into multiplicity through processio.

  • Eriugena translates Dionysius’ paradox:

- God is neither one nor many.
- The divine unitas manifests as multiplicitas without division.
  • Mystical ascent: Moving from the many back to the One is the soul’s goal.


3. Apophatic and Cataphatic Theology

42. Negatio (from Greek ἀπόφασις, apophasis)


Meaning: “Negation.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • In Dionysius, negation is the only way to describe God (the via negativa).

  • Eriugena systematizes this: every divine predicate must be:

1. Affirmed (cataphasis – "God is light")
2. Denied (apophasis – "God is not light")
3. Super-negated ("God is beyond light").

43. Affirmatio (from Greek κατάφασις, kataphasis)


Meaning: “Affirmation.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • Affirmation is the lower form of theology, where divine names (bonitas, vita, lux) are provisional truths.

  • Eriugena’s innovation: Affirmations must always be negated, since God is beyond being.


44. Transcendere (from Greek ὑπερβαίνειν, hyperbainein)


Meaning: “To transcend.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • In Dionysius, transcendence means going beyond all concepts (excessus).

  • Eriugena’s translation strengthens this:

- God transcends all things (superessentialis).
- The soul transcends knowledge into mystical ignorance (docta ignorantia).

45. Ineffabilis (from Greek ἄρρητος, arrhētos)


Meaning: “Inexpressible.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • Eriugena’s key concept: God is beyond speech, and the highest theology is silence (silentium).

  • Mystical paradox:

- The more one knows, the less one can say.
- The highest name for God is no name.

4. Theophany and Divine Manifestation

46. Theophania (from Greek θεοφάνεια, theophaneia)


Meaning: “Manifestation of God.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • In Dionysius, all things manifest traces of the divine.

  • Eriugena expands this:

- Creation is itself a theophany.
- The material world participates in divine being.

47. Participatio (from Greek μέθεξις, methexis)


Meaning: “Participation.”
Neoplatonic Significance:
  • Being is not self-sufficient—it participates in the divine.

  • Eriugena’s adaptation:

- The soul participates in God through knowledge.
- All beings “borrow” their existence from the divine.

Conclusion: Eriugena’s Neoplatonic Vision

Eriugena’s translation preserves and transforms Neoplatonic thought. His Latin emphasizes:
1. Procession and Return (processio → reditus).
2. Divine Transcendence (unitas, negatio).
3. Mystical Ascent (excessus, silentium).
4. Participation (theophania, participatio).

Thus, his translation is not just linguistic—it is a philosophical statement, shaping medieval Christian Neoplatonism.

Glossary of Latin Terms in Eriugena’s Mystical Theology with Biblical Significance

This glossary highlights Latin terms in Eriugena’s translation that carry biblical significance, showing how they connect Scripture with Neoplatonic and mystical theology. Many of these terms link Eriugena’s vision of God and creation to biblical themes of light, darkness, divine names, and mystical ascent.

1. Divine Names and Attributes

48. Dominus (from Greek Κύριος, Kyrios)


Meaning: “Lord.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Kyrios is the Greek Septuagint translation of YHWH, emphasizing divine sovereignty.

  • In Philippians 2:11, “Jesus Christ is Lord” (Dominus Iesus Christus).

  • Eriugena’s usage: As in Dionysius, divine lordship is beyond human kingship—God rules not by force but by causal overflow.


49. Lux (from Greek φῶς, phōs)


Meaning: “Light.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Genesis 1:3: “Fiat lux” (Let there be light).

  • John 1:4-5: “The light shines in the darkness.”

  • Eriugena’s use: Light signifies divine illumination, but he also follows Dionysius in negating it—God is beyond even the concept of light (superlucens).


50. Verbum (from Greek Λόγος, Logos)


Meaning: “Word.”
Biblical Significance:
  • John 1:1: “In principio erat Verbum” (In the beginning was the Word).

  • Eriugena’s theology:

- The Logos is the divine reason through which all things exist.
- However, God is beyond even the Word (verbum eius non est).

51. Vita (from Greek ζωή, zōē)


Meaning: “Life.”
Biblical Significance:
  • John 14:6: “Ego sum via et veritas et vita” (I am the way, the truth, and the life).

  • In Dionysius and Eriugena, God is the source of all life, but life as we know it must be transcended.


52. Sapientia (from Greek σοφία, sophia)


Meaning: “Wisdom.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Proverbs 8:22: “Dominus possedit me in initio viarum suarum” (The Lord created me at the beginning of His ways).

  • Eriugena’s use: Sapientia divina is one of God’s names, yet it is ultimately negated in apophatic theology.


2. Creation and Theophany

53. Principium (from Greek ἀρχή, archē)


Meaning: “Beginning.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Genesis 1:1: “In principio creavit Deus caelum et terram” (In the beginning, God created the heavens and the earth).

  • John 1:1: “In principio erat Verbum” (In the beginning was the Word).

  • Eriugena follows Dionysius in seeing principium as a timeless source—the divine beyond time.


54. Nomen (from Greek ὄνομα, onoma)


Meaning: “Name.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Exodus 3:14: “Ego sum qui sum” (I AM WHO AM).

  • In Scripture, naming something gives power over it, but God’s name is beyond comprehension.

  • Eriugena’s use: Nomen divinum (divine name) must be affirmed, negated, and transcended.


55. Gloria (from Greek δόξα, doxa)


Meaning: “Glory.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Exodus 33:18: “Ostende mihi gloriam tuam” (Show me your glory).

  • John 17:5: “Clarifica me, Pater, apud temetipsum gloria” (Glorify me, Father, with the glory I had before the world began).

  • Eriugena follows Dionysius in treating gloria as a divine manifestation—yet God is beyond even glory.


3. Darkness and the Mystical Ascent

56. Caligo (from Greek γνόφος, gnophos)


Meaning: “Divine darkness.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Exodus 20:21: “Moyses accessit ad caliginem in qua erat Deus” (Moses entered the darkness where God was).

  • Eriugena’s use: Caligo is not evil darkness, but the unknowable presence of God.


57. Tenebrae (from Greek σκότος, skotos)


Meaning: “Darkness.”
Biblical Significance:
  • John 1:5: “Lux in tenebris lucet” (The light shines in the darkness).

  • Eriugena distinguishes between:

- Caligo (mystical divine darkness).
- Tenebrae (darkness of ignorance or absence of light).

58. Ignis (from Greek πῦρ, pyr)


Meaning: “Fire.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Exodus 3:2: “Apparuit ei Dominus in flamma ignis” (The Lord appeared to him in a flame of fire).

  • Hebrews 12:29: “Deus noster ignis consumens est” (Our God is a consuming fire).

  • Eriugena follows Dionysius in seeing divine fire as both a destructive force and a purifying illumination.


4. Salvation and Divine Union

59. Via (from Greek ὁδός, hodos)


Meaning: “The way.”
Biblical Significance:
  • John 14:6: “Ego sum via” (I am the way).

  • Eriugena integrates this: The via negativa (negative way) is the path to divine union.


60. Pax (from Greek εἰρήνη, eirēnē)


Meaning: “Peace.”
Biblical Significance:
  • John 14:27: “Pacem meam do vobis” (My peace I give you).

  • Eriugena’s mystical theology: Pax divina is beyond human peace, found in the soul’s union with God.


61. Misericordia (from Greek ἔλεος, eleos)


Meaning: “Mercy.”
Biblical Significance:
  • Luke 1:50: “Misericordia eius in progenies et progenies” (His mercy is from generation to generation).

  • Eriugena follows Dionysius: Misericordia divina is the overflowing of God’s goodness, yet God transcends even mercy.


Conclusion: Eriugena’s Biblical-Neoplatonic Synthesis


  • Divine Names (Dominus, Lux, Sapientia) must be affirmed, negated, and transcended.

  • Creation (Principium, Nomen, Gloria) is a theophany, but God is beyond manifestation.

  • Mystical Ascent (Caligo, Tenebrae, Ignis) leads to union beyond light and darkness.

  • Salvation (Via, Pax, Misericordia) occurs through returning to the divine source (reditus).


By linking Biblical revelation with Neoplatonic metaphysics, Eriugena reinterprets Scripture through Dionysian apophaticism, shaping medieval mystical theology.

Give me a sentence by sentence grammatical commentary explaining features of the words and sentences like case endings and verb conjugations, and other difficult grammatical aspects for beginner students of latin. INCIPIT LIBER DE MYSTICA THEOLOGIA.
CAPITULUM I. De mystica theologia.
Trinitas superessentialis, et superdeus, et superoptime Christianorum inspector theosophiae, dirige nos in mysticorum eloquiorum superincognitum et superlucentem et sublimissimum verticem, ubi nova et absoluta et inconversibilia theologiae mysteria, secundum superlucentem absconduntur occulte docentis silentii caliginem, in obscurissimo, quod est supermanifestissimum, supersplendentem, et in qua omne relucet, et invisibilium superbonorum splendoribus superimplentem invisibiles intellectus. Mihi quidem haec opto. Tu autem, o amice Timothee, circa mysticas speculationes corroborato itinere et sensus desere, et intellectuales operationes, et sensibilia, et invisibilia, et omne non ens, et ens; et ad unitatem, ut possibile, inscius restituere ipsius, qui est super omnem essentiam et scientiam. Ea enim teipso et omnibus immensurabili et absoluto pure mentis excessu ad superessentialem divinarum tenebrarum radium, omnia deserens et ab omnibus absolutus ascendes. His autem, vide, quomodo nemo indoctorum auscultet. Indoctos autem dico, in his, quae sunt, conformatos, et nihil super existentia superessentialiter esse imaginantes. Sed his quidem hi, quos videre ea, quae secundum seipsos est, scientia oportet tenebras latibulum ejus. Si autem super hos sunt divinae in mysteria introductiones, quid quis dixerit de aliis ardentibus, quicunque omnibus superpositam causam ex ipsis in his, quae sunt, novissimis characterizant, et nihil eam superare aiunt ab ipsis fictarum impietatum et multiformium formationum? In ipsa etiam oportet omnes existentium ponere et affirmare positiones, veluti omnium causa, et omnes eas potentius negare, tanquam super omnia superexistente, et non aestimari depulsiones oppositas esse intentionibus, sed multo prius ipsam super privationes esse, quae est super omnem ablationem et positionem. Sic igitur divinus Bartholomaeus ait, et multam theologiam esse, et minimam, et evangelium latum, et magnum, et iterum correptum. Mihi videtur supernaturaliter intelligens, quia et multiloqua est optima omnium causa, et breviloqua simul, et sine verbo, quomodo neque verbum, neque intelligentiam habet, eo quod omnibus ipsa superessentialiter superposita est, et solis incircumvelate et vere manifesta, polluta omnia et immunda transgredientibus, et omnem omnium sanctarum summitatum ascensionem superascendentibus, et omnia divina lumina et sonos et verba caelestia superantibus, et in caliginem occidentibus, ubi vere est, ut eloquia aiunt, omnium summitas. Etenim non simpliciter divinus ipse Moyses primus mundari jubetur, et iterum ab his, qui tales non sunt, segregari, et post omnem purgationem audit multivocas tubas, et videt luminaria multa aperte fulgurantia, et multum fusos radios. Deinde multis segregatur, et cum electis sacerdotibus in summitatem divinarum ascensionum praecurrit: et si eis sic manentibus fit Deo, contemplatur vero non ipsum, invisibilis enim, sed locum ubi stetit. Hoc autem arbitror significare divinissima et sublimissima visibilium et intelligibilium, ypotheticos quosdam esse sermones, subjectorum omnia superanti, per quae super omnem intelligentiam ipsius praesentia ostenditur, intelligibilibus summitatibus sanctissimorum ejus locorum supergrediens: et quod ipsis absolvitur visibilibus et videntibus, et in caliginem ignorantiae occidit vere mysticam, per quam docet omnes gnosticas receptiones, in qua omne relucet, et invisibili innascitur omnis, qui est in omnium summitate, et a nullo, neque a seipso, neque altero, omnino autem ignoto omni scientia in otio per id quod melius est intellectus, et nihil cognoscendum super animum sic cognoscentium.

CAPITULUM II. Quomodo oportet et uniri et hymnos referre omnium causali et super omnia.
Juxta hanc nos fieri superlucentem oramus caliginem, et per invisibilitatem et ignorantiam videre et cognoscere ipsum super Deum et scientiam. Hoc non videre et scire, idipsum est vere videre et cognoscere, superessentialem superessentialiter laudare per omnium existentium ablationem, sicut per seipsum naturale agalma facientes, auferunt ea, quae superadjecta sunt, pura occulti visione vetantia, et ipsam in seipsa ablatione sola occultam manifestant formam. Oportet autem, ut arbitror, ablationes in contrarium positionibus laudare. Etenim illas quidem a praestantissimis inchoantes, et per media in novissima descendentes, apponimus. Hinc vero a novissimis ad principalissimas ascensiones facientes, omnia auferimus, ut incircumvelate cognoscamus illam ignorantiam, ab omnibus ignorantibus in omnibus existentibus circumvelatam, et superessentialem illam videamus caliginem, ab omni in existentibus luce occultatam.

CAPITULUM III. Quae sunt καταφατικαί theologiae, quae αποφατικαί.
In theologicis igitur characteribus potentissima affirmativae theologiae laudavimus, quomodo divina et optima natura unica dicitur, quomodo triadica, quae secundum ipsam dicta et paternitas, et filiolitas, quid vult declarare in spiritu theologia, quomodo ex immateriali et impartibili optimo in corde bonitatis germinata sunt lumina, et quomodo ipsius in ipso et in seipsis et inter se invicem coaeternae in germinatione mansionis servaverunt reditum, quomodo superessentialis Jesus humanis naturalibus veritatibus essentia factus est, et quaecunque alia ab eloquiis expressa sunt secundum theologicos characteres. In eo autem qui est de divinis nominibus, quomodo optimus nominatur, quomodo ων, quomodo vita, et sapientia, et virtus, et quaecunque alia intelligibilis sunt divinae nominationis. In symbolica vero theologia, quae sint a sensibilibus in divina transnominationes, quae divinae formae, quae divinae figurae, et partes, et organa, qui divini loci et mundi, qui furores, quae tristitiae et maniae, quae ebrietates et crapulae, quae juramenta, quae execrationes, qui somni, quae vigiliae, et quaecunque aliae symbolicae sunt divinae similitudinis sacre figuratae formationes. Et te arbitror considerasse, quomodo verbis copiosiora magis sunt novissima primis. Etenim habere theologicos characteres, et divinorum nominum reserationem breviorem verbis esse symbolica theologia. Quoniam quidem quantum ad superiora respicimus, tantum verba contemplationibus invisibilium coartantur: sicut et nunc in ipsam super intellectum occidentes caliginem, non brevem sermonem, sed sermonis defectum et nominationis inveniemus. Et ibi quidem desursum ad novissima descendens sermo, juxta quantitatem ejus, quae est universaliter ad proportionalem multitudinem, inventus est. Nunc autem ab his, quae deorsum sunt, ad superpositum ascendens, secundum mensuram invii corripitur, et post omne invium totus sine voce erit, et totus adunabitur sono carenti. Quare autem omnino, inquis, ex praestantissimo ponentes divinas positiones, a novissimis inchoamus divinam ablationem? Quia quid super omnem ponentes positionem, ex magis ipsi cognatiori conditionalem affirmationem oportuit ponere: quod autem super omnem ablationem auferentes, ex magis ipso distantibus auferre. An non magis est vita et bonitas, quam aer et lapis? Et magis non crapulae, et non maniae, quam non dicitur neque intelligitur?

CAPITULUM IV. Quia nihil sensibilium omnis sensibilis per excellentiam causalis.
Dicamus igitur sic: Omnium causa, et super omnia ens, neque carens essentia est, neque carens vita, neque irrationabilis est, neque insensualis, neque corpus est, neque figura, neque species, neque qualitatem, aut quantitatem, aut tumorem habet, neque in loco est, neque videtur, neque tactum sensibilem habet, neque sentitur, neque sensibilis est, neque inordinationem habet, neque perturbationem a passionibus materialibus commota, neque impotens est sensibilibus succumbens casibus, neque indigens est lucis, neque mutationem, aut corruptionem, aut partitionem, aut privationem, aut fluxum, neque aliud quid sensibilium est, neque habet

CAPITULUM V. Quia nihil intelligibilium omnis intelligibilis per excellentiam causalis.
Iterum autem ascendentes dicamus, ων neque anima est, neque intellectus, neque phantasiam, aut opinionem, aut verbum, aut intelligentiam habet, neque ratio est, neque intelligentia, neque dicitur, neque intelligitur, neque numerus est, neque ordo, neque magnitudo, neque parvitas, neque aequalitas, neque similitudo aut dissimilitudo, neque stat, neque movetur, neque silentium ducit, neque habet virtutem, neque virtus est, neque lux, neque vita est, neque hostia est, neque seculum, neque tempus, neque tactus est ejus intelligibilis, neque scientia, neque veritas est, neque regnum, neque sapientia, neque unum, neque unitas, neque deitas, aut bonitas, neque spiritus est, sicut nos scimus, neque filiolitas, neque paternitas, neque aliud quid nobis aut alicui existentium cognitum, neque quid non existentium, neque quid existentium est, neque existentia eam cognoscunt, an ipsa sit, neque ipsa cognoscit existentia, an existentia sunt, neque verbum ejus est, neque nomen, neque scientia, neque tenebrae est, neque lumen, neque error, neque veritas, neque est ejus universaliter positio, neque ablatio, sed eorum, quae post eam sunt, positiones et ablationes facientes, ipsam neque auferimus, neque ponimus, quoniam et super omnem positionem est perfecta et singularis omnium causa, super omnem ablationem excellentia omnium simpliciter perfectione, et summitas omnium.

Explicit liber de mystica theologia.

Here is a sentence-by-sentence grammatical commentary on the Latin passage, explaining case endings, verb conjugations, syntax, and difficult grammatical constructions for beginner Latin students.

Sentence 1


Trinitas superessentialis, et superdeus, et superoptime Christianorum inspector theosophiae, dirige nos in mysticorum eloquiorum superincognitum et superlucentem et sublimissimum verticem

1. Trinitas superessentialis → Trinitas (feminine singular nominative) is the subject; superessentialis is an adjective meaning "beyond essence" (genitive or nominative but here agreeing with Trinitas in nominative).
2. et superdeus, et superoptime → These are additional descriptions of the Trinity: superdeus (nominative singular, “beyond-God”) and superoptime (vocative singular, "most supremely excellent one").
3. Christianorum inspector theosophiae → Christianorum (genitive plural, "of Christians") modifies inspector (nominative singular, "overseer"), and theosophiae (genitive singular, "of divine wisdom").
4. dirige nos → Dirige is a second-person singular imperative of dirigere (“to direct, guide”); nos is accusative plural, the direct object (“guide us”).
5. in mysticorum eloquiorum superincognitum et superlucentem et sublimissimum verticem
- in + accusative (verticem, singular accusative, “summit” or “peak”) indicates motion toward.
- mysticorum eloquiorum is genitive plural (“of mystical utterances”).
- superincognitum (accusative singular, “beyond-unknown”), superlucentem (accusative singular participle, “beyond-shining”), and sublimissimum (accusative singular, superlative of sublimis, “most sublime”) all modify verticem.

📌 Summary: This is a vocative prayer sentence, addressing the "Trinity beyond essence" and asking it to guide the speaker to the summit of mystical utterances.

Sentence 2


ubi nova et absoluta et inconversibilia theologiae mysteria, secundum superlucentem absconduntur occulte docentis silentii caliginem

1. ubi → A relative adverb, meaning "where".
2. nova et absoluta et inconversibilia → These are nominative plural adjectives modifying mysteria (“new, absolute, and unchangeable”).
3. theologiae mysteria → Mysteria (nominative plural, "mysteries") is the subject, with theologiae (genitive singular, “of theology”) modifying it.
4. secundum superlucentem → Secundum + accusative means “according to”; superlucentem (accusative singular, "beyond-shining") agrees with an implied noun.
5. absconduntur → 3rd person plural passive indicative of abscondere ("to hide"), meaning "are hidden."
6. occulte → Adverb ("secretly").
7. docentis silentii caliginem
- docentis (genitive singular participle, “of the teaching one”) modifies silentii ("of silence").
- caliginem (accusative singular, “darkness”) is the direct object of absconduntur.

📌 Summary: This describes a hidden, transcendent realm where new and absolute mysteries of theology are secretly concealed in the shining darkness of silent teaching.

Sentence 3


Mihi quidem haec opto.

1. Mihi → Dative singular of ego, functioning as an indirect object ("for myself").
2. quidem → Adverb meaning “indeed.”
3. haec → Accusative plural neuter demonstrative pronoun ("these things"), the direct object of opto.
4. opto → 1st person singular present indicative of optare ("to wish, desire").

📌 Summary: "Indeed, I desire these things for myself." This is a simple declarative sentence.

Sentence 4


Tu autem, o amice Timothee, circa mysticas speculationes corroborato itinere et sensus desere

1. Tu autem → Tu is nominative singular ("you"), and autem is a contrastive conjunction ("but").
2. o amice Timothee → Amice is vocative singular of amicus ("friend"), and Timothee is the vocative form of "Timothy."
3. circa mysticas speculationes → Circa + accusative plural (speculationes, “contemplations”) means "concerning mystical contemplations".
4. corroborato itinere → Corroborato (ablative singular perfect participle, “having been strengthened”) modifies itinere (ablative singular, “journey”).
5. et sensus desere
- Sensus (accusative plural, “senses”) is the direct object.
- Desere is second-person singular imperative of deserere ("to abandon").

📌 Summary: "But you, O friend Timothy, having strengthened your journey concerning mystical contemplations, abandon your senses." This admonition tells Timothy to leave behind sensory perception to pursue mystical ascent.

Sentence 5


et intellectuales operationes, et sensibilia, et invisibilia, et omne non ens, et ens

1. et... et... et... → This repetition (polysyndeton) emphasizes abandoning multiple aspects of reality.
2. intellectuales operationes → Operationes is accusative plural ("operations"), modified by intellectuales ("intellectual").
3. sensibilia, et invisibilia → Accusative plural neuter ("sensible things and invisible things").
4. omne non ens, et ens →
- Omne is accusative singular neuter ("every").
- Non ens means “non-being” (philosophical term from Neoplatonism).
- Ens means “being” (from esse, "to be").

📌 Summary: "And abandon intellectual operations, sensible things, invisible things, all non-being, and being itself." This is a philosophical paradox, instructing one to abandon both existence and non-existence to reach God.

Sentence 6


et ad unitatem, ut possibile, inscius restituere ipsius, qui est super omnem essentiam et scientiam.

1. et ad unitatem → Ad + accusative (unitatem, "unity") indicates movement toward unity.
2. ut possibile → Ut + subjunctive expresses purpose; possibile (nominative neuter singular, "as much as possible").
3. inscius restituere →
- Inscius (nominative singular, "unknowing") modifies the implied subject.
- Restituere (present active infinitive, "to return, restore").
4. ipsius → Genitive singular pronoun ("of Him").
5. qui est super omnem essentiam et scientiam
- Qui (nominative singular, "who") introduces a relative clause.
- Super + accusative (omnem essentiam et scientiam, "above all essence and knowledge").

📌 Summary: "And unknowingly return to unity, as much as possible, to Him who is beyond all essence and knowledge." This echoes Neoplatonic mystical ascent.

Conclusion


Each sentence follows a structured pattern, with:
  • Vocative appeals (O amice Timothee).

  • Imperative commands (dirige, desere).

  • Abstract philosophical nouns (unitatem, caliginem).

  • Neoplatonic oppositions (ens et non ens).


📖 This passage reflects Dionysian mysticism, urging the reader to ascend beyond knowledge into divine unknowing.

Sentence 7


Ea enim teipso et omnibus immensurabili et absoluto pure mentis excessu ad superessentialem divinarum tenebrarum radium, omnia deserens et ab omnibus absolutus ascendes.

1. Ea enim → Ea is accusative plural neuter ("these things"), referring back to the previous discussion. Enim is a postpositive conjunction meaning "for" or "indeed."
2. teipso et omnibus →
- Teipso is ablative singular of the reflexive pronoun ("yourself").
- Omnibus (ablative plural, "from all things") is governed by absolutus below.
3. immensurabili et absoluto pure mentis excessu →
- Immensurabili and absoluto are ablative singular adjectives, modifying excessu ("immeasurable and absolute").
- Pure is an adverb ("purely").
- Mentis excessu (ablative singular, “by an excess of mind”) is an ablative of means, indicating how the action is done.
4. ad superessentialem divinarum tenebrarum radium →
- Ad + accusative (radium, "ray") shows motion towards something.
- Superessentialem (accusative singular, “beyond-essence”) modifies radium.
- Divinarum tenebrarum is genitive plural, meaning "of the divine darkness."
5. omnia deserens et ab omnibus absolutus ascendes. →
- Omnia deserens (nominative singular present participle, “abandoning all things”) describes the subject.
- Ab omnibus absolutus (ablative absolute, "having been freed from all things").
- Ascendes is second-person singular future active indicative, meaning "you will ascend."

📌 Summary: "For by an immeasurable and absolute excess of mind, you will purely ascend, abandoning all things and freed from all things, to the beyond-essential ray of divine darkness."
Neoplatonic Idea: The soul’s ascent beyond being into divine darkness.

Sentence 8


His autem, vide, quomodo nemo indoctorum auscultet.

1. His autem → His (ablative plural neuter, "to these things"), governed by auscultet below.
2. vide → Imperative of videre, meaning “see!” (command).
3. quomodo → Interrogative adverb meaning "how."
4. nemo indoctorum auscultet →
- Nemo is nominative singular ("no one").
- Indoctorum is genitive plural ("of the unlearned").
- Auscultet is third-person singular present subjunctive active of auscultare ("to listen, heed"), likely in a jussive sense (“let no one listen”).

📌 Summary: "See how none of the unlearned may listen."
Mystical Exclusivity: Only the initiated can understand divine knowledge.

Sentence 9


Indoctos autem dico, in his, quae sunt, conformatos, et nihil super existentia superessentialiter esse imaginantes.

1. Indoctos autem dico →
- Indoctos (accusative plural, "the unlearned") is the direct object of dico ("I say").
- Autem (postpositive) emphasizes contrast.
2. in his, quae sunt, conformatos →
- In + ablative plural (his, "in these things") means "conformed to these things".
- Quae sunt ("which exist") is a relative clause modifying his.
- Conformatos (accusative plural perfect passive participle, “having been conformed”) modifies indoctos.
3. et nihil super existentia superessentialiter esse imaginantes. →
- Nihil (accusative singular neuter, “nothing”) is the direct object of imaginantes.
- Super existentia ("beyond existence") is accusative plural.
- Superessentialiter (adverb, "in a beyond-essential manner").
- Esse (infinitive, "to be") is the verb of indirect speech.
- Imaginantes (accusative plural present participle, “imagining”) describes indoctos.

📌 Summary: "I call unlearned those who are conformed to these existing things and imagine nothing to exist beyond existence itself in a beyond-essential manner."
Mystical Meaning: The unlearned are trapped in existence and cannot conceive of what lies beyond.

Sentence 10


Sed his quidem hi, quos videre ea, quae secundum seipsos est, scientia oportet tenebras latibulum ejus.

1. Sed his quidem hi →
- Sed introduces contrast.
- His (dative plural, "for these") sets up an indirect object.
- Quidem emphasizes contrast (“but indeed”).
- Hi (nominative plural, "these people") is the subject of oportet.
2. quos videre ea →
- Quos (accusative plural relative pronoun, "whom") is the direct object of videre.
- Videre (infinitive, "to see") is part of the indirect statement governed by oportet.
- Ea (accusative plural neuter, "those things") is the object of videre.
3. quae secundum seipsos est, scientia →
- Quae is a relative pronoun referring to ea.
- Secundum seipsos ("according to themselves") modifies scientia (nominative singular, "knowledge").
- Est (third-person singular present active indicative) links scientia to quae.
4. oportet tenebras latibulum ejus →
- Oportet (impersonal verb, “it is necessary”) governs an indirect statement.
- Tenebras (accusative plural, “darkness”) is the direct object.
- Latibulum (accusative singular, "hiding place") is appositive to tenebras.
- Ejus (genitive singular, "of him") modifies latibulum.

📌 Summary: "But for these, indeed, those whom knowledge (which is according to themselves) requires to see, it is necessary to perceive darkness as his hiding place."
Biblical Allusion: Exodus 20:21 (Moses entered divine darkness).

Conclusion: Key Grammar Features


📖 Case Usage:
  • Frequent ablative absolutes (ab omnibus absolutus = “having been freed from all things”).

  • Accusative subjects of indirect speech (nihil... imaginantes).

  • Genitives of possession (latibulum ejus = “his hiding place”).


📖 Verb Forms:
  • Imperatives (vide!, desere!).

  • Future tense (ascendes).

  • Passive constructions (absconduntur = "are hidden").


📖 Syntax & Word Order:
  • Neoplatonic abstractions (contrast of tenebrae vs. caligo).

  • Jussive subjunctives (nemo indoctorum auscultet).


📖 Theological & Mystical Meaning:
  • Unknowing leads to divine ascent (mentis excessu).

  • Separation from existence is necessary (omnia deserens).

  • Darkness as divine mystery (tenebras latibulum ejus).


📌 This passage reflects Dionysian apophatic theology, requiring mystical ascent beyond reason into divine unknowing.

Sentence 11


Si autem super hos sunt divinae in mysteria introductiones, quid quis dixerit de aliis ardentibus, quicunque omnibus superpositam causam ex ipsis in his, quae sunt, novissimis characterizant, et nihil eam superare aiunt ab ipsis fictarum impietatum et multiformium formationum?

1. Si autem super hos sunt divinae in mysteria introductiones
- Si (subordinating conjunction, "if") introduces a conditional clause.
- Autem (postpositive particle, "however").
- Super hos (accusative plural, "above these ones"), referring to those capable of mystical ascent.
- Sunt (third-person plural present indicative of esse, "are").
- Divinae introductiones (nominative plural, "divine introductions"), the subject.
- In mysteria (accusative plural, "into the mysteries") governed by introductiones ("introductions into the mysteries").

2. quid quis dixerit de aliis ardentibus
- Quid (accusative singular neuter interrogative pronoun, "what").
- Quis (nominative singular interrogative pronoun, "who").
- Dixerit (third-person singular perfect subjunctive active of dicere, "would say"), indicating indirect speech.
- De aliis ardentibus (ablative plural, "about the others who are burning").
- Ardentibus is a present participle (ablative plural), from ardere ("to burn"), likely metaphorical for those passionate in their ignorance.

3. quicunque omnibus superpositam causam ex ipsis in his, quae sunt, novissimis characterizant
- Quicunque (nominative plural relative pronoun, "whoever").
- Omnibus (dative plural, "to all").
- Superpositam causam (accusative singular, "the cause placed above"), object of characterizant.
- Ex ipsis (ablative plural, "from them").
- In his, quae sunt (ablative plural, "in these things which exist").
- Novissimis (dative plural or ablative plural, "to the lowest things" or "in the lowest things").
- Characterizant (third-person plural present active indicative, "they characterize").

4. et nihil eam superare aiunt ab ipsis fictarum impietatum et multiformium formationum?
- Et nihil eam superare → Indirect statement governed by aiunt ("they say").
- Nihil (accusative singular neuter, "nothing"), object of superare.
- Eam (accusative singular feminine pronoun, "her" = "the cause").
- Superare (present active infinitive of superare, "to surpass").
- Aiunt (third-person plural present active indicative, "they say").
- Ab ipsis (ablative plural, "by them").
- Fictarum impietatum et multiformium formationum (genitive plural, "of feigned impieties and of multiform formations").

📌 Summary: "But if there are divine introductions into the mysteries beyond these, what would someone say about others who burn (in ignorance), those who characterize the transcendent cause in the lowest things that exist, and who say that nothing surpasses it, based on their own false impieties and multiform fabrications?"
💡 Mystical Meaning: Ignorant people mischaracterize the divine cause, lowering it into a realm of impiety and illusion.

Sentence 12


In ipsa etiam oportet omnes existentium ponere et affirmare positiones, veluti omnium causa, et omnes eas potentius negare, tanquam super omnia superexistente.

1. In ipsa etiam
- In ipsa (ablative singular feminine, "in itself").
- Etiam ("also").

2. oportet omnes existentium ponere et affirmare positiones
- Oportet (impersonal verb, "it is necessary"), governing indirect speech.
- Omnes existentium → Omnes (accusative plural, "all people") and existentium (genitive plural participle, "of existing ones").
- Ponere et affirmare (present infinitives, "to place and to affirm").
- Positiones (accusative plural noun, "positions").

3. veluti omnium causa
- Veluti ("as if").
- Omnium causa (genitive singular, "the cause of all").

4. et omnes eas potentius negare, tanquam super omnia superexistente
- Omnes eas (accusative plural, "all these things").
- Potentius (comparative adverb, "more powerfully").
- Negare (infinitive, "to deny").
- Tanquam ("as though").
- Super omnia superexistente (ablative absolute, "as surpassing all things").

📌 Summary: "It is necessary to affirm and establish all positions of existence as if they were the cause of everything, and then to deny them even more powerfully, as though the divine surpasses all things."
💡 Neoplatonic Influence: Reality must be affirmed, then negated, in the ascent beyond all being.

Sentence 13


Sic igitur divinus Bartholomaeus ait, et multam theologiam esse, et minimam, et evangelium latum, et magnum, et iterum correptum.

1. Sic igitur divinus Bartholomaeus ait
- Sic igitur ("Thus, therefore").
- Divinus Bartholomaeus (nominative singular, "the divine Bartholomew").
- Ait (third-person singular present active indicative, "says").

2. et multam theologiam esse, et minimam
- Multam theologiam (accusative singular, "great theology").
- Esse (infinitive, "to be"), part of indirect speech.
- Et minimam (accusative singular adjective, "and the smallest").

3. et evangelium latum, et magnum, et iterum correptum
- Evangelium (accusative singular, "the Gospel").
- Latum (accusative singular adjective, "broad").
- Magnum (accusative singular adjective, "great").
- Iterum ("again").
- Correptum (accusative singular perfect participle, "abridged").

📌 Summary: "Thus, the divine Bartholomew says that theology is both vast and minimal, and that the Gospel is broad and great, yet also abridged."
💡 Mystical Paradox: Divine knowledge is at once immense and contracted.

Conclusion: Latin Grammar Insights


  • Case Usage:

- Ablative absolutes (super omnia superexistente).
- Genitive participles (existentium, "of those existing").
  • Verb Forms:

- Indirect speech (oportet omnes ponere).
- Comparative adverbs (potentius).
  • Syntax & Word Order:

- Contrasts (multam theologiam esse et minimam).

📌 Theological Message: Truth is hidden in paradox; ascent requires both affirmation and negation.
🔥 Neoplatonic & Apophatic Thought: Knowledge of God requires transcending all affirmations into mystical silence.
